# Internet history timeline: ARPANET to the World Wide Web

[References](https://www.livescience.com/references)

By [Jesse Emspak](https://www.livescience.com/author/jesse-emspak), [Kim Ann Zimmermann](https://www.livescience.com/author/kim-ann-zimmermann)   published

The internet history timeline shows how today's vast network evolved from the initial concept

In internet history, credit for the initial concept that developed into the World Wide Web is typically given to Leonard Kleinrock. In 1961, he wrote about ARPANET, the predecessor of the internet, in a paper entitled "Information Flow in Large Communication Nets."

According to the journal [Management and Business Review](https://mbrjournal.com/2021/01/26/leonard-kleinrock-internet-pioneer/) (MBR), Kleinrock, along with other innovators such as J.C.R. Licklider, the first director of the Information Processing Technology Office (IPTO), provided the backbone for the ubiquitous stream of emails, media, Facebook postings and tweets that are now shared online every day.

Related articles

[Firewall: Definition, technology and facts](https://www.livescience.com/firewall)

[Latency: Definition, measurement and testing](https://www.livescience.com/latency)

[What is cyberwarfare?](https://www.livescience.com/what-is-cyber-warfare)

The precursor to the [internet](https://www.livescience.com/internet) was jumpstarted in the early days of the [history of computers](https://www.livescience.com/20718-computer-history.html) , in 1969 with the U.S. Defense Department's Advanced Research Projects Agency Network (ARPANET), according to the journal [American Scientist](https://www.jstor.org/stable/27856002). ARPA-funded researchers developed many of the protocols used for internet communication today. This timeline offers a brief history of the internet’s evolution:

Article continues below 

### Internet timeline: 1960s

**1965:** Two computers at MIT Lincoln Lab communicate with one another using packet-switching technology.

**1968:** Beranek and Newman, Inc. (BBN) unveils the final version of the Interface Message Processor (IMP) specifications. BBN wins ARPANET contract.

**1969:** On Oct. 29, UCLA’s Network Measurement Center, Stanford Research Institute (SRI), University of California-Santa Barbara and University of Utah install nodes. The first message is "LO," which was an attempt by student Charles Kline to "LOGIN" to the SRI computer from the university. However, the message was unable to be completed because the SRI system crashed.

![Internet nodes](https://cdn.mos.cms.futurecdn.net/bUwFNAcF3Rg8qxyK3bZyeb.png)

Internet nodes are network connection points. Each line represents a path between two nodes in the internet backbone.   (Image credit: The Opte Project)

### 1970–1980

**1972:** BBN’s Ray Tomlinson introduces network email. The Internet Working Group (INWG) forms to address need for establishing standard protocols.

Sign up for the Live Science daily newsletter now

Get the world’s most fascinating discoveries delivered straight to your inbox.

**1973:** Global networking becomes a reality as the University College of London (England) and Royal Radar Establishment (Norway) connect to ARPANET. The term internet is born.

**1974:** The first Internet Service Provider (ISP) is born with the introduction of a commercial version of ARPANET, known as Telenet.

**1974:** Vinton Cerf and Bob Kahn (the duo said by many to be the Fathers of the Internet) publish "A Protocol for Packet Network Interconnection," which details the design of [TCP](https://www.livescience.com/tcp-ip).

**1976:** Queen Elizabeth II hits the “send button” on her first email.

**1979:** USENET forms to host news and discussion groups.

### 1980–1990

**1981:** The National Science Foundation (NSF) provided a grant to establish the Computer Science Network (CSNET) to provide networking services to university computer scientists.

**1982:** Transmission Control Protocol (TCP) and Internet Protocol (IP), as the protocol suite, commonly known as TCP/IP, emerge as the protocol for ARPANET. This results in the fledgling definition of the internet as connected TCP/IP internets. TCP/IP remains the standard protocol for the internet.

**1983:** The Domain Name System (DNS) establishes the familiar .edu, .gov, .com, .mil, .org, .net, and .int system for naming websites. This is easier to remember than the previous designation for websites, such as 123.456.789.10.

**1984:** William Gibson, author of "Neuromancer," is the first to use the term "cyberspace."

**1985:** Symbolics.com, the website for Symbolics Computer Corp. in Massachusetts, becomes the first registered domain.

**1986:** The National Science Foundation’s NSFNET goes online to connected supercomputer centers at 56,000 bits per second — the speed of a typical dial-up computer modem. Over time the network speeds up and regional research and education networks, supported in part by NSF, are connected to the NSFNET backbone — effectively expanding the Internet throughout the United States. The NSFNET was essentially a network of networks that connected academic users along with the ARPANET.

**1987:** The number of hosts on the internet exceeds 20,000. Cisco ships its first router.

**1989:** World.std.com becomes the first commercial provider of dial-up access to the internet.

![World Wide Web](https://cdn.mos.cms.futurecdn.net/td62D5ELJoAcFPW2GjX7cK.jpg)

The internet is older than the World Wide Web (WWW). (Image credit: Getty Images)

### 1990–2000

**1990:** Tim Berners-Lee, a scientist at CERN, the European Organization for Nuclear Research, develops HyperText Markup Language (HTML). This technology continues to have a large impact on how we navigate and view the internet today.

**1991:** CERN introduces the [World Wide Web](https://www.livescience.com/world-wide-web) to the public.

**1992:** The first audio and video are distributed over the internet. The phrase "surfing the internet" is popularized.

**1993:** The number of websites reaches 600 and the White House and United Nations go online. Marc Andreesen develops the Mosaic Web browser at the University of Illinois, Champaign-Urbana. The number of computers connected to NSFNET grows from 2,000 in 1985 to more than 2 million in 1993. The National Science Foundation leads an effort to outline a new internet architecture that would support the burgeoning commercial use of the network.

**1994:** Netscape Communications is born. Microsoft creates a Web browser for Windows 95.

**1994:** Yahoo! is created by Jerry Yang and David Filo, two electrical [engineering](https://www.livescience.com/47499-what-is-engineering.html) graduate students at Stanford University. The site was originally called "Jerry and David's Guide to the World Wide Web." The company was later incorporated in March 1995.

**1995:** Compuserve, America Online and Prodigy begin to provide internet access. Amazon.com, Craigslist and eBay go live. The original NSFNET backbone is decommissioned as the internet’s transformation to a commercial enterprise is largely completed.

**1995:** The first online dating site, Match.com, launches.

**1996:** The browser war, primarily between the two major players Microsoft and Netscape, heats up. CNET buys tv.com for $15,000.

**1996:** A 3D animation dubbed "[The Dancing Baby](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-5x5OXfe9KY)" becomes one of the first viral videos.

**1997:** Netflix is founded by Reed Hastings and Marc Randolph as a company that sends users DVDs by mail.

![People watching laptop](https://cdn.mos.cms.futurecdn.net/zcPnhHyJpasjTGuQixWJR7.jpg)

In 2022, Netflix has over 200 million subscribers.  (Image credit: Getty Images)

**1997:** PC makers can remove or hide Microsoft’s internet software on new versions of Windows 95, thanks to a settlement with the Justice Department. Netscape announces that its browser will be free.

**1998:** The Google search engine is born, changing the way users engage with the internet.

**1998:** The Internet Protocol version 6 introduced, to allow for future growth of Internet Addresses. The current most widely used protocol is version 4. IPv4 uses 32-bit addresses allowing for 4.3 billion unique addresses; IPv6, with 128-bit addresses, will allow 3.4 x 1038 unique addresses, or 340 trillion trillion trillion.

**1999:** AOL buys Netscape. Peer-to-peer file sharing becomes a reality as Napster arrives on the Internet, much to the displeasure of the music industry.

### 2000–2010

**2000:** The dot-com bubble bursts. Websites such as Yahoo! and eBay are hit by a large-scale denial of service attack, highlighting the vulnerability of the Internet. AOL merges with Time Warner

**2001:** A federal judge shuts down Napster, ruling that it must find a way to stop users from sharing copyrighted material before it can go back online.

**2003:** The SQL Slammer worm spread worldwide in just 10 minutes. Myspace, Skype and the Safari Web browser debut.

**2003:** The blog publishing platform WordPress is launched.

**2004:** Facebook goes online and the era of social networking begins. Mozilla unveils the Mozilla Firefox browser.

**2005:** YouTube.com launches. The social news site Reddit is also founded.

**2006:** AOL changes its business model, offering most services for free and relying on advertising to generate revenue. The Internet Governance Forum meets for the first time.

**2006:** Twitter launches. The company's founder, Jack Dorsey, sends out the very first tweet: "just setting up my twttr."

**2009:** The internet marks its 40th anniversary.

### 2010–2020

**2010:** Facebook reaches 400 million active users.

**2010:** The social media sites Pinterest and Instagram are launched.

**2011:** Twitter and Facebook play a large role in the Middle East revolts.

**2012:** President Barack Obama's administration announces its opposition to major parts of the Stop Online Piracy Act and the Protect Intellectual Property Act, which would have enacted broad new rules requiring internet service providers to police copyrighted content. The successful push to stop the bill, involving technology companies such as Google and nonprofit organizations including Wikipedia and the Electronic Frontier Foundation, is considered a victory for sites such as YouTube that depend on user-generated content, as well as "fair use" on the internet.

**2013:** Edward Snowden, a former CIA employee and National Security Agency (NSA) contractor, reveals that the NSA had in place a monitoring program capable of tapping the communications of thousands of people, including U.S. citizens.

**2013:** Fifty-one percent of U.S. adults report that they bank online, according to a survey conducted by the Pew Research Center.

![Online banking](https://cdn.mos.cms.futurecdn.net/EZSCX4ZuiWMtTUm34XYNLa.jpg)

The first online banking experiments took place in the 1980s.  (Image credit: Getty Images)

**2015:** Instagram, the photo-sharing site, reaches 400 million users, outpacing Twitter, which would go on to reach 316 million users by the middle of the same year.

**2016:** Google unveils Google Assistant, a voice-activated personal assistant program, marking the entry of the internet giant into the "smart" computerized assistant marketplace. Google joins Amazon's Alexa, Siri from Apple, and Cortana from Microsoft.

**2018:** There is a significant rise in internet-enabled devices. An increase in the Internet of Things (IoT) sees around seven billion devices by the end of the year.

**2019:** Fifth–generation ([5G](https://www.livescience.com/65959-5g-network.html)) networks are launched, enabling speedier internet connection on some wireless devices.

### 2020–2022

**2021:** By January 2021, there are 4.66 billion people connected to the internet. This is more than half of the global population.

**2022:** Low–Earth orbit satellite internet is closer to reality. By early January 2022, [SpaceX](https://www.livescience.com/tag/spacex) launches more than 1,900 [Starlink](https://www.livescience.com/starlink) satellites overall. The constellation is now providing broadband service in select areas around the world.

### Additional resources

To find out more about the SpaceX satellite internet project, you can watch [this video](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HwyXo6T7jC4) about the mission. Additionally, to read an interview with Leonard Kleinrock, visit the [Communications of the ACM website](https://cacm.acm.org/magazines/2019/11/240360-an-interview-with-leonard-kleinrock/fulltext).

Title: Timeline of Internet Milestones: Key Events and Developments - JMC Study Hub
![](https://jmcstudyhub.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/05/JMC-Logo-2022.jpg)

## Timeline of Internet Milestones: Key Events and Developments

![Picture of Dr. Ranjan Kumar](https://jmcstudyhub.com/wp-content/uploads/2024/08/2-150x150.jpg)

# Dr. Ranjan Kumar

![The history of the internet and communication technologies](https://jmcstudyhub.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/01/Quick-Revision-3.jpg)

## About JMC Study Hub

**JMC Study Hub** is India’s first largest and dedicated learning platform of Journalism and Mass Communication.

**Email :** support@jmcstudyhub.com

The history of the internet and communication technologies is a story of innovation and transformation. From the early days of ARPANET to the establishment of the World Wide Web and modern platforms like Google, these developments have reshaped how we communicate and access information. This blog post provides a structured timeline of major milestones, complete with descriptions and additional insights into each event.

| **S.No** | **Event** | **Year** | **Key Highlights** | **Impact** |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| 1 | **ARPANET’s first public demonstration** | **1972** | Showcased packet-switching technology, laying the foundation for modern networking. | Marked the start of computer-to-computer communication, leading to the internet’s creation. |
| 2 | **Internet protocol suite (TCP/IP) standardized** | **1982** | Established the standard communication protocol for data exchange on the internet. | Enabled global connectivity and interoperability between devices. |
| 3 | **Domain Name System (DNS) introduced** | **1983** | Simplified internet navigation by assigning human-readable domain names to IP addresses. | Made internet usage more user-friendly and accessible globally. |
| 4 | **World Wide Web (W3C) founded** | **1994** | Tim Berners-Lee established the W3C to set standards for the development of the web. | Created universal protocols for building and accessing websites. |
| 5 | **Voice over Internet Protocol (VoIP) launched** | **1995** | Enabled voice communication over the internet, replacing traditional telephony systems. | Revolutionized global communication, lowering costs and enabling virtual meetings. |
| 6 | **Google founded as a research project** | **1996** | Larry Page and Sergey Brin developed Google at Stanford University, focusing on search algorithms. | Transformed how information is accessed and organized on the internet. |
| 7 | **Wi-Fi introduced (802.11 standard)** | **1997** | Provided wireless networking capabilities, enabling devices to connect without physical cables. | Boosted mobile internet access and device connectivity worldwide. |
| 8 | **Social Media Revolution began (Six Degrees)** | **1997** | Launched as the first social media platform, pioneering online interaction and networking. | Set the foundation for platforms like Facebook, Twitter, and LinkedIn. |
| 9 | **Broadband Internet widely adopted** | **2000** | Faster internet connections enabled streaming, online gaming, and video conferencing. | Enhanced content delivery and transformed digital entertainment and communication. |
| 10 | **Mobile Internet growth with 3G launch** | **2001** | Introduced faster mobile internet speeds, enabling advanced mobile applications and browsing. | Paved the way for smartphones and mobile-first services. |

This *quicky* presents a timeline of key milestones in internet and communication technologies, showcasing their evolution and impact. From ARPANET to Google and Wi-Fi, these advancements have transformed connectivity, enabling faster communication and reshaping industries.

*We value your feedback! Share your thoughts in the comments and follow ‘JMC Study Hub’ on social media for more updates and resources.*

Founder & Educator

# Internet History 1962 to 1992

 ![](_media/posterhistoryofinternet1962-1992.jpg) 



 
> In the Beginning, ARPA created the ARPANET.   
>   
> And the ARPANET was without form and void.   
>   
> And darkness was upon the deep.   
>   
> And the spirit of ARPA moved upon the face of the network and ARPA said, 'Let there be a protocol,' and there was a protocol. And ARPA saw that it was good.   
>   
> And ARPA said, 'Let there be more protocols,' and it was so. And ARPA saw that it was good.   
>   
> And ARPA said, 'Let there be more networks,' and it was so." Danny Cohen

This Internet Timeline begins in 1962, before the word 'Internet' is invented. The world’s 10,000 computers are primitive, although they cost hundreds of thousands of dollars. They have only a few thousand words of magnetic core memory, and programming them is far from easy.

Domestically, data communication over the phone lines is an AT&T monopoly. The 'Picturephone' of 1939, shown again at the New York World's Fair in 1964, is still AT&T’s answer to the future of worldwide communications.

But the four-year old Advanced Research Projects Agency (ARPA) of the U.S. Department of Defense, a future-oriented funder of ‘high-risk, high-gain’ research, lays the groundwork for what becomes the ARPANET and, much later, the Internet.

By 1992, when this timeline ends,

* the Internet has one million hosts
* the ARPANET has ceased to exist
* computers are nine orders of magnitude faster
* network bandwidth is twenty million times greater

---

The [Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet "Internet") originated in the efforts of scientists and engineers to build and interconnect [computer networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_network "Computer network"). The [Internet Protocol Suite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite "Internet protocol suite"), the set of rules used to communicate between networks and devices on the Internet, arose from research and development in the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") and involved international collaboration, particularly with researchers in the [United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom") and [France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France "France").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Abbatep3-1)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:3-2)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:4-3)

[Computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science") was an emerging discipline in the late 1950s that began to consider [time-sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-sharing "Time-sharing") between computer users, and later, the possibility of achieving this over [wide area networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_area_network "Wide area network"). [J. C. R. Licklider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._C._R._Licklider "J. C. R. Licklider") articulated the idea of a universal network at the [Information Processing Techniques Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_Processing_Techniques_Office "Information Processing Techniques Office") (IPTO) of the United States [Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") (DoD) [Advanced Research Projects Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") (ARPA). Independently, [Paul Baran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Baran "Paul Baran") at the [RAND Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAND_Corporation "RAND Corporation") proposed a distributed network based on data in message blocks in the early 1960s, and [Donald Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Davies "Donald Davies") conceived of [packet switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_switching "Packet switching") in 1965 at the [National Physical Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory_(United_Kingdom) "National Physical Laboratory (United Kingdom)") (NPL), proposing a national commercial data network in the United Kingdom.

ARPA awarded contracts in 1969 for the development of the [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET") project, directed by [Robert Taylor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Taylor_(computer_scientist) "Robert Taylor (computer scientist)") and managed by [Lawrence Roberts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lawrence_Roberts_(scientist) "Lawrence Roberts (scientist)"). ARPANET adopted the packet switching technology proposed by Davies and sought input from Baran. The network of [Interface Message Processors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interface_Message_Processor "Interface Message Processor") (IMPs) was built by a team at [Bolt, Beranek, and Newman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolt,_Beranek,_and_Newman "Bolt, Beranek, and Newman"), with the design and specification led by [Bob Kahn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bob_Kahn "Bob Kahn"). The host-to-host protocol was specified mainly by graduate students, led by [Steve Crocker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Crocker "Steve Crocker") at [UCLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles "University of California, Los Angeles"), along with [Jon Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Postel "Jon Postel") and others. The ARPANET expanded rapidly across the United States with connections to the United Kingdom and Norway.

Several [early packet-switched networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_packet-switched_networks "List of packet-switched networks") emerged in the 1970s which researched and provided [data networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_network "Computer network"). [Louis Pouzin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Pouzin "Louis Pouzin") and [Hubert Zimmermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubert_Zimmermann "Hubert Zimmermann") pioneered a simplified end-to-end approach to [internetworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internetworking "Internetworking") at the [IRIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/INRIA "INRIA"). [Peter Kirstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_T._Kirstein "Peter T. Kirstein") put internetworking into practice at [University College London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_College_London "University College London") in 1973. [Bob Metcalfe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Metcalfe "Robert Metcalfe") developed the theory and practice behind [Ethernet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethernet "Ethernet") and the [PARC Universal Packet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARC_Universal_Packet "PARC Universal Packet"). ARPA initiatives and the [International Network Working Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Network_Working_Group "International Network Working Group") developed and refined ideas for internetworking, in which multiple separate networks could be joined into a _network of networks_. [Vint Cerf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vint_Cerf "Vint Cerf"), now at [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University"), and Bob Kahn, now at DARPA, published their research on internetworking in 1974. Through the [Internet Experiment Note](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Experiment_Note "Internet Experiment Note") series and later [RFCs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments") this evolved into the [Transmission Control Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol "Transmission Control Protocol") (TCP) and [Internet Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Protocol "Internet Protocol") (IP), two protocols of the [Internet protocol suite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite "Internet protocol suite"). The design reflected concepts pioneered in the French [CYCLADES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CYCLADES "CYCLADES") project directed by Louis Pouzin. The development of packet switching networks was complemented by mathematical work in the 1970s by [Leonard Kleinrock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonard_Kleinrock "Leonard Kleinrock") at UCLA.

In the late 1970s, national and international [public data networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_data_network "Public data network") emerged based on the [X.25](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.25 "X.25") protocol, designed by [Rémi Després](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%A9mi_Despr%C3%A9s "Rémi Després") and others. In the United States, the [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation") (NSF) funded national [supercomputing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercomputer "Supercomputer") centers at several universities in the United States, and provided interconnectivity in 1986 with the [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") project, thus creating network access to these supercomputer sites for research and academic organizations in the United States. International connections to NSFNET, the emergence of architecture such as the [Domain Name System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_Name_System "Domain Name System"), and the [adoption of TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite#Adoption "Internet protocol suite") on existing networks in the United States and around the world marked the beginnings of the [Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet "Internet").[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-4)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:2-6) Commercial [Internet service providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") (ISPs) emerged in 1989 in the United States and Australia.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-7) Limited private connections to parts of the Internet by officially commercial entities emerged in several American cities by late 1989 and 1990.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-8) The optical backbone of the NSFNET was decommissioned in 1995, removing the last restrictions on the use of the Internet to carry commercial traffic, as traffic transitioned to optical networks managed by Sprint, MCI and AT&T in the United States.

Research at [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN") in [Switzerland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Switzerland "Switzerland") by the British computer scientist [Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee "Tim Berners-Lee") in 1989–90 resulted in the [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web"), linking [hypertext](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypertext "Hypertext") documents into an information system, accessible from any [node](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Node_(networking) "Node (networking)") on the network.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-9) The dramatic expansion of the capacity of the Internet, enabled by the advent of [wave division multiplexing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength-division_multiplexing "Wavelength-division multiplexing") (WDM) and the rollout of [fiber optic cables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber-optic_cable "Fiber-optic cable") in the mid-1990s, had a revolutionary impact on culture, commerce, and technology. This made possible the rise of near-instant communication by [electronic mail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email"), [instant messaging](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instant_messaging "Instant messaging"), [voice over Internet Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voice_over_Internet_Protocol "Voice over Internet Protocol") (VoIP) telephone calls, [video chat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_chat "Video chat"), and the World Wide Web with its [discussion forums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discussion_forums "Discussion forums"), [blogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blogs "Blogs"), [social networking services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_networking_service "Social networking service"), and [online shopping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_shopping "Online shopping") sites. Increasing amounts of data are transmitted at higher and higher speeds over [fiber-optic networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber-optic_communication "Fiber-optic communication") operating at 1 [Gbit/s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gbits/sec "Gbits/sec"), 10 Gbit/s, and 800 Gbit/s by 2019.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-10) The Internet's takeover of the global communication landscape was rapid in historical terms: it only communicated 1% of the information flowing through two-way [telecommunications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications "Telecommunications") networks in the year 1993, 51% by 2000, and more than 97% of the telecommunicated information by 2007.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-HilbertLopez2011-11) The Internet continues to grow, driven by ever greater amounts of online information, commerce, entertainment, and [social networking services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_networking_service "Social networking service"). However, the future of the global network may be shaped by regional differences.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20181015-12)

## Foundations

### Precursors

#### Telegraphy

The practice of transmitting messages between two different places through an electromagnetic medium dates back to the [electrical telegraph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_telegraph "Electrical telegraph") in the late 19th century, which was the first fully digital communication system. [Radiotelegraphy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiotelegraphy "Radiotelegraphy") began to be used commercially in the early 20th century. [Telex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telex "Telex") became an operational [teleprinter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleprinter "Teleprinter") service in the 1930s. Such systems were limited to [point-to-point communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point-to-point_(telecommunications) "Point-to-point (telecommunications)") between two [end devices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End_system "End system").

#### Information theory

Fundamental theoretical work in [telecommunications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications "Telecommunications") technology was developed by [Harry Nyquist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry_Nyquist "Harry Nyquist") and [Ralph Hartley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Hartley "Ralph Hartley") in the 1920s. [Information theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory "Information theory"), as enunciated by [Claude Shannon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_Shannon "Claude Shannon") in 1948, provided a firm theoretical [underpinning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Underpinning "Underpinning") to understand the trade-offs between [signal-to-noise ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio "Signal-to-noise ratio"), [bandwidth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bandwidth_(computing) "Bandwidth (computing)"), and error-free [transmission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_(telecommunications) "Transmission (telecommunications)") in the presence of [noise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noise "Noise").

#### Computers and modems

Early fixed-program [computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer "Computer") in the 1940s were operated manually by entering small programs via [switches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Switch "Switch") in order to load and run a series of programs. As [transistor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transistor "Transistor") technology evolved in the 1950s, [central processing units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_processing_unit "Central processing unit") and user [terminals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_terminal "Computer terminal") came into use by 1955. The [mainframe computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainframe_computer "Mainframe computer") model was devised, and [modems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modem "Modem"), such as the [Bell 101](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_101_modem "Bell 101 modem"), allowed [digital data](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_data "Digital data") to be transmitted over regular unconditioned [telephone lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_line "Telephone line") at low speeds by the late 1950s. These technologies made it possible to exchange data between [remote computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Remote_desktop_software "Remote desktop software"). However, a fixed-line link was still necessary; the point-to-point communication model did not allow for direct communication between any two arbitrary systems. In addition, the applications were specific and not general purpose. Examples included [SAGE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semi-Automatic_Ground_Environment "Semi-Automatic Ground Environment") (1958) and [SABRE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabre_(travel_reservation_system) "Sabre (travel reservation system)") (1960).

#### Time-sharing

[Christopher Strachey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Strachey "Christopher Strachey"), who became [Oxford University's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Oxford "University of Oxford") first Professor of [Computation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computation "Computation"), filed a [patent application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patent_application "Patent application") in the United Kingdom for [time-sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-sharing "Time-sharing") in February 1959.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-13)[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:13-14) In June that year, he gave a paper "Time Sharing in Large Fast Computers" at the [UNESCO Information Processing Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Federation_for_Information_Processing#History "International Federation for Information Processing") in Paris where he passed the concept on to [J. C. R. Licklider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._C._R._Licklider "J. C. R. Licklider").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ctsspg-15)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-16) Licklider, a vice president at [Bolt Beranek and Newman, Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBN_Technologies "BBN Technologies") (BBN), promoted the idea of time-sharing as an alternative to [batch processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batch_processing "Batch processing").[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:13-14)[John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)"), at [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology "Massachusetts Institute of Technology"), wrote a memo in 1959 that broadened the concept of time sharing to encompass multiple interactive user sessions, which resulted in the [Compatible Time-Sharing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatible_Time-Sharing_System "Compatible Time-Sharing System") (CTSS) implemented at MIT. Other multi-user mainframe systems developed, such as [PLATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PLATO_(computer_system) "PLATO (computer system)") at the [University of Illinois Chicago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Illinois_Chicago "University of Illinois Chicago").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-17) In the early 1960, the [Advanced Research Projects Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency "Advanced Research Projects Agency") (ARPA) of the [United States Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") funded further research into time-sharing at MIT through [Project MAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_MAC "Project MAC").

### Inspiration

J. C. R. Licklider, while working at BBN, proposed a computer network in his March 1960 paper _[Man–Computer Symbiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Man%E2%80%93Computer\_Symbiosis "Man–Computer Symbiosis")_:[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-18)

> A network of such centers, connected to one another by wide-band communication lines [...] the functions of present-day libraries together with anticipated advances in information storage and retrieval and symbiotic functions suggested earlier in this paper

In August 1962, Licklider and Welden Clark published the paper "On-Line Man-Computer Communication"[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-19) which was one of the first descriptions of a networked future.

In October 1962, Licklider was hired by [Jack Ruina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_Ruina "Jack Ruina") as director of the newly established [Information Processing Techniques Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_Processing_Techniques_Office "Information Processing Techniques Office") (IPTO) within ARPA, with a mandate to interconnect the United States Department of Defense's main computers at [Cheyenne Mountain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cheyenne_Mountain "Cheyenne Mountain"), the Pentagon, and SAC HQ. There he formed an informal group within DARPA to further computer research. He began by writing memos in 1963 describing a distributed network to the IPTO staff, whom he called "Members and Affiliates of the [Intergalactic Computer Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intergalactic_Computer_Network "Intergalactic Computer Network")".[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-20)

Although he left the IPTO in 1964, five years before the ARPANET went live, it was his vision of universal networking that provided the impetus for one of his successors, [Robert Taylor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Taylor_(computer_scientist) "Robert Taylor (computer scientist)"), to initiate the ARPANET development. Licklider later returned to lead the IPTO in 1973 for two years.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-21)

### Packet switching

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/The_idea_of_the_data_packet_%28Baran%2C_1964%29-en.svg/250px-The_idea_of_the_data_packet_%28Baran%2C_1964%29-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_idea_of_the_data_packet_(Baran,_1964)-en.svg)

The "message block", designed by [Paul Baran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Baran "Paul Baran") in 1962 and refined in 1964, is the first proposal of a [data packet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_packet "Data packet").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:1-22)[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23)

Main article: [Packet switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_switching "Packet switching")

The infrastructure for [telephone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone "Telephone") systems at the time was based on [circuit switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circuit_switching "Circuit switching"), which requires pre-allocation of a dedicated communication line for the duration of the call. [Telegram services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telegram_services "Telegram services") had developed [store and forward](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Store_and_forward "Store and forward") telecommunication techniques. [Western Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Union "Western Union")'s Automatic Telegraph Switching System [Plan 55-A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plan_55-A "Plan 55-A") was based on [message switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Message_switching "Message switching"). The U.S. military's [AUTODIN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AUTODIN "AUTODIN") network became operational in 1962. These systems, like SAGE and SBRE, still required rigid routing structures that were prone to [single point of failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single_point_of_failure "Single point of failure").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-24)

The technology was considered vulnerable for strategic and military use because there were no alternative paths for the communication in case of a broken link. In the early 1960s, [Paul Baran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Baran "Paul Baran") of the [RAND Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAND_Corporation "RAND Corporation") produced a study of survivable networks for the U.S. military in the event of nuclear war.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-25)[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-26) Information would be transmitted across a "distributed" network, divided into what he called "message blocks".[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey6.1a-27)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-28)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:5a-29)[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-30) Baran's design was intended for high-speed [digital communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_communication "Digital communication") of voice messages using low-cost hardware; it was not implemented.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:5a2-31)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey6.1a2-32)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-33)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-34)

In addition to being prone to a single point of failure, existing telegraphic techniques were inefficient and inflexible. Beginning in 1965 [Donald Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Davies "Donald Davies"), at the [National Physical Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory,_UK "National Physical Laboratory, UK") in the United Kingdom, independently developed a similar proposal of the concept, designed for high-speed [data communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_communication "Data communication") in [computer networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_network "Computer network"), which he called [packet switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_switching "Packet switching"), the term that would ultimately be adopted.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:62-35)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-A_Hey,_G_P%C3%A1pay2-36)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-37)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-38)

Packet switching is a technique for transmitting computer data by splitting it into very short, standardized chunks, attaching routing information to each of these chunks, and transmitting them independently through a computer network. It provides better bandwidth utilization than traditional circuit-switching used for telephony, and enables the connection of computers with different transmission and receive rates. It is a distinct concept to message switching.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-39)

## Networks that led to the Internet

See also: [List of packet-switched networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_packet-switched_networks "List of packet-switched networks")

### NPL network

Main article: [NPL network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NPL_network "NPL network")

Following discussions with [J. C. R. Licklider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._C._R._Licklider "J. C. R. Licklider") in 1965, [Donald Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Davies "Donald Davies") became interested in [data communications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_communications "Data communications") for computer networks.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Roberts1978-40)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Roberts1995-41) Later that year, at the [National Physical Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory_(United_Kingdom) "National Physical Laboratory (United Kingdom)") (NPL) in the United Kingdom, Davies designed and proposed a national commercial data network based on packet switching.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-42) The following year, he described the use of "switching nodes" to act as [routers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Router_(computing) "Router (computing)") in a digital communication network.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-43)[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-44) The proposal was not taken up nationally but he produced a design for a local network to serve the needs of the NPL and prove the feasibility of packet switching using high-speed data transmission.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-K.G._Coffman_&_A.M._Odlyzco-45)[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-B._Steil,_Council_on_Foreign_Relations-46) To deal with packet permutations (due to dynamically updated route preferences) and to datagram losses (unavoidable when fast sources send to a slow destinations), he assumed that "all users of the network will provide themselves with some kind of error control",[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-47) thus inventing what came to be known as the [end-to-end principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End-to-end_principle "End-to-end principle"). In 1967, he and his team were the first to use the term 'protocol' in a modern data-commutation context.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-48)

In 1968,[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-49) Davies began building the Mark I packet-switched network to meet the needs of his multidisciplinary laboratory and prove the technology under operational conditions.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-C._Hempstead,_W._Worthington2-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-BBC_Technology-51) The network's development was described at a 1968 conference.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-52)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-53) Elements of the network became operational in early 1969,[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-C._Hempstead,_W._Worthington2-50)[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:722-54) the first implementation of packet switching,[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:22-55)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-56) and the NPL network was the first to use high-speed links.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:32-57) Many other packet switching networks built in the 1970s were similar "in nearly all respects" to Davies' original 1965 design.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Roberts1978-40) The Mark II version which operated from 1973 used a layered protocol architecture.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:32-57) In 1977, there were roughly 30 computers, 30 peripherals and 100 VDU terminals all able to interact through the NPL Network.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-58) The NPL team carried out [simulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simulation "Simulation") work on wide-area packet networks, including [datagrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Datagram "Datagram") and [congestion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_congestion "Network congestion"); and research into [internetworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internetworking "Internetworking") and [secure communications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secure_communications "Secure communications").[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-C._Hempstead,_W._Worthington2-50)[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:82-59)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey-60) The network was replaced in 1986.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:32-57)

### ARPANET

Main article: [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET")

Robert Taylor was promoted to the head of the [Information Processing Techniques Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_Processing_Techniques_Office "Information Processing Techniques Office") (IPTO) at [Advanced Research Projects Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency "Advanced Research Projects Agency") (ARPA) in 1966. He intended to realize [Licklider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._C._R._Licklider "J. C. R. Licklider")'s ideas of an interconnected networking system.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-61) As part of the IPTO's role, three network terminals had been installed: one for [System Development Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/System_Development_Corporation "System Development Corporation") in [Santa Monica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Santa_Monica,_California "Santa Monica, California"), one for [Project Genie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_Genie "Project Genie") at [University of California, Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley "University of California, Berkeley"), and one for the [Compatible Time-Sharing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatible_Time-Sharing_System "Compatible Time-Sharing System") project at [Massachusetts Institute of Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology "Massachusetts Institute of Technology") (MIT).[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Markoff_1999-62) Taylor's identified need for networking became obvious from the waste of resources apparent to him.

> For each of these three terminals, I had three different sets of user commands. So if I was talking online with someone at S.D.C. and I wanted to talk to someone I knew at Berkeley or M.I.T. about this, I had to get up from the S.D.C. terminal, go over and log into the other terminal and get in touch with them.... I said, oh man, it's obvious what to do: If you have these three terminals, there ought to be one terminal that goes anywhere you want to go where you have interactive computing. That idea is the ARPAnet.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Markoff_1999-62)

Bringing in [Larry Roberts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lawrence_Roberts_(scientist) "Lawrence Roberts (scientist)") from MIT in January 1967, he initiated a project to build such a network. Roberts and Thomas Merrill had been researching computer [time-sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-sharing "Time-sharing") over [wide area networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_area_network "Wide area network") (WANs).[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-63) Wide area networks emerged during the late 1950s and became established during the 1960s. At the first ACM [Symposium on Operating Systems Principles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symposium_on_Operating_Systems_Principles "Symposium on Operating Systems Principles") in October 1967, Roberts presented a proposal for the "ARPA net", based on [Wesley Clark's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wesley_A._Clark "Wesley A. Clark") idea to use [Interface Message Processors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interface_Message_Processor "Interface Message Processor") (IMP) to create a [message switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Message_switching "Message switching") network.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-64)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-65)[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-66) At the conference, [Roger Scantlebury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Scantlebury "Roger Scantlebury") presented [Donald Davies'](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Davies "Donald Davies") work on a hierarchical digital communications network using [packet switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_switching "Packet switching") and referenced the work of [Paul Baran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Baran "Paul Baran") at [RAND](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RAND_Corporation "RAND Corporation"). Roberts incorporated the packet switching concepts proposed by Davies into the ARPANET design. He upgraded the proposed communications speed from 2.4 kbit/s to 50 kbit/s and sought input from Baran.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Abbate20002-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-68)

ARPA awarded the contract to build the network to [Bolt Beranek & Newman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolt_Beranek_%26_Newman "Bolt Beranek & Newman"). The "IMP guys", led by [Frank Heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Heart "Frank Heart") and [Bob Kahn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bob_Kahn "Bob Kahn"), developed the routing, flow control, software design and network control.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Roberts1978-40)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-F.E._Froehlich,_A._Kent-69) The first ARPANET link was established between the Network Measurement Center at the [University of California, Los Angeles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles "University of California, Los Angeles") (UCLA) [Henry Samueli School of Engineering and Applied Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Samueli_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science "Henry Samueli School of Engineering and Applied Science") led by [Leonard Kleinrock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonard_Kleinrock "Leonard Kleinrock"), and the NLS system at [Stanford Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_Research_Institute "Stanford Research Institute") (SRI) led by [Douglas Engelbart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Engelbart "Douglas Engelbart") in [Menlo Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Menlo_Park,_California "Menlo Park, California"), California at 22:30 hours on October 29, 1969. With reference to the actions of the graduate and undergraduate students working with the IMPs, Kleinrock said:[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-70)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-How_ARPANET_Works-71)

> "We set up a telephone connection between us and the guys at SRI ...", Kleinrock ... said in an interview: "We typed the L and we asked on the phone,
> 
> "Do you see the L?""Yes, we see the L," came the response.We typed the O, and we asked, "Do you see the O.""Yes, we see the O."Then we typed the G, and the system crashed ...
> Yet a revolution had begun" ....[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-72)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NetValley-73)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Stamps_of_Azerbaijan%2C_2004-683.jpg/250px-Stamps_of_Azerbaijan%2C_2004-683.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stamps_of_Azerbaijan,_2004-683.jpg)

Postage stamp of Azerbaijan (2004): 35 Years of the Internet, 1969–2004

By December 1969, a four-node network was connected by adding the Culler-Fried Interactive Mathematics Center at the [University of California, Santa Barbara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara "University of California, Santa Barbara") followed by the [University of Utah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Utah "University of Utah") Graphics Department.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-74) In the same year, Taylor helped fund [ALOHAnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ALOHAnet "ALOHAnet"), a system designed by professor [Norman Abramson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norman_Abramson "Norman Abramson") and others at the [University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Hawai%CA%BBi_at_M%C4%81noa "University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa") that transmitted data by radio between seven computers on four islands on [Hawaii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hawaii "Hawaii").[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-75)

[Steve Crocker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Crocker "Steve Crocker"), a graduate student at UCLA, formed the "Network Working Group" in 1969. Working with [Jon Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Postel "Jon Postel") and others,[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-76) he initiated and managed the [Request for Comments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments") (RFC) process, which is still used today for proposing and distributing contributions. RFC 1, entitled "Host Software", was written by Crocker and published on April 7, 1969. The protocol for establishing links between network sites in the ARPANET, the [Network Control Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Control_Protocol_(ARPANET) "Network Control Protocol (ARPANET)") (NCP), was completed in 1970. These early years were documented in the 1972 film [Computer Networks: The Heralds of Resource Sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_Networks:_The_Heralds_of_Resource_Sharing "Computer Networks: The Heralds of Resource Sharing").

Roberts presented the idea of packet switching to the communication professionals, and faced anger and hostility. Before ARPANET was operating, they argued that the router buffers would quickly run out. After the ARPANET was operating, they argued packet switching would never be economic without the government subsidy. Baran had faced the same rejection and thus failed to convince the military into constructing a packet switching network.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-77)[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-78)

Early international collaborations via the ARPANET were sparse. Connections were made in 1973 to Norway ([NORSAR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NORSAR "NORSAR")),[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-79) via a satellite link at the [Tanum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanum_Municipality "Tanum Municipality") Earth Station in Sweden, and to [Peter Kirstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_T._Kirstein "Peter T. Kirstein")'s research group at [University College London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_College_London "University College London"), which provided a gateway to [British academic networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_in_the_United_Kingdom#History "Internet in the United Kingdom"), the first international heterogenous [resource sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_resource "Shared resource") network.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:9-80) Throughout the 1970s, Leonard Kleinrock developed the mathematical theory to model and measure the performance of packet-switching technology, building on his earlier work on the application of [queueing theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queueing_theory "Queueing theory") to message switching systems.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:122-81)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-82)[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:43-83) By 1981, the number of hosts had grown to 213.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-84) The ARPANET became the technical core of what would become the Internet, and a primary tool in developing the technologies used.

### CYCLADES

Main article: [CYCLADES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CYCLADES "CYCLADES")

[CYCLADES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CYCLADES "CYCLADES") was a French research network designed and directed by [Louis Pouzin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Pouzin "Louis Pouzin").[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:34-85) In 1972, he began implementing his ideas to build on the work of Donald Davies and explore alternatives to the early ARPANET design.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey2-86)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-87) His goal was to enable [internetworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internetworking "Internetworking"), which he called a "catenet".[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-88) This was the first network to implement the [end-to-end principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/End-to-end_principle "End-to-end principle") by making the hosts responsible for reliable delivery of data, rather than the network itself, using [unreliable datagrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Datagrams "Datagrams").[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Green_2010_31-89)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Bennett20092-90) Concepts implemented in this network influenced the initial proposal of the Transmission Control Program,[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:7-91)[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-RFC_675-92) and were reflected in the later [TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TCP/IP "TCP/IP") architecture.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:8-93)[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-94)

### X.25 and public data networks

Main articles: [X.25](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.25 "X.25") and [public data network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_data_network "Public data network")

1974 interview with [Arthur C. Clarke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke "Arthur C. Clarke") by the [Australian Broadcasting Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation "Australian Broadcasting Corporation"), in which he describes a future of ubiquitous networked personal computers

Based on international research initiatives, particularly the contributions of [Rémi Després](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%A9mi_Despr%C3%A9s "Rémi Després"), packet switching network standards were developed by the CCITT, which is now the [International Telegraph and Telephone Consultative Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telegraph_and_Telephone_Consultative_Committee "International Telegraph and Telephone Consultative Committee") (ITU-T), in the form of [X.25](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.25 "X.25") and related standards.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:11-95)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:12-96) X.25 is built on the concept of [virtual circuits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virtual_circuit "Virtual circuit") emulating traditional telephone connections. The initial ITU Standard on X.25 was approved in March 1976.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-97) Existing networks, such as [Telenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telenet "Telenet") in the United States adopted X.25 as well as new [public data networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_data_network "Public data network"), such as [DATAPAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DATAPAC "DATAPAC") in Canada and [TRANSPAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_switching#TRANSPAC "Packet switching") in France.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:11-95)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:12-96) The protocol formed the basis for the SERCnet network between British academic and research sites, which became [JANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JANET "JANET") in the 1980s, the United Kingdom's high-speed [national research and education network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_research_and_education_network "National research and education network") (NREN).

The [British Post Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post_Office_Telecommunications "Post Office Telecommunications"), [Western Union International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Union "Western Union"), and [Tymnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tymnet "Tymnet") collaborated to create the first international packet-switched network, referred to as the [International Packet Switched Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Packet_Switched_Service "International Packet Switched Service") (IPSS), in 1978. This network grew from Europe and the US to cover Canada, Hong Kong, and Australia by 1981. By the 1990s it provided a worldwide networking infrastructure.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-98)

X.25 was supplemented by the [X.75](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.75 "X.75") protocol which enabled internetworking between national PTT networks in Europe and commercial networks in North America.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-99)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-100)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-101)

Unlike the ARPANET and its protocols, X.25 was available for business use. In 1979, CompuServe became the first service to offer commercial [electronic mail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-mail "E-mail") capabilities and technical support to personal computer users. The company broke new ground again in 1980 as the first to offer [real-time chat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_chat "Online chat") with its [CB Simulator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CB_Simulator "CB Simulator"). Telenet offered its Telemail electronic mail service, which was also targeted to enterprise use unlike the network mail system of the ARPANET. Other major dial-in networks were [America Online](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/America_Online "America Online") (AOL) and [Prodigy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prodigy_(ISP) "Prodigy (ISP)") that also provided communications, content, and entertainment features.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-102)

[Bulletin board system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board_system "Bulletin board system") (BBS) networks also provided on-line access, such as [FidoNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FidoNet "FidoNet") which was popular amongst hobbyist computer users, many of them [hackers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hacker "Hacker") and [amateur radio operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator "Amateur radio operator").[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Many of these public data networks went on to adopt TCP/IP and formed the infrastructure of the early Internet.

### UUCP and Usenet

Main articles: [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") and [Usenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet")

In 1979, two students at [Duke University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duke_University "Duke University"), [Tom Truscott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Truscott "Tom Truscott") and [Jim Ellis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jim_Ellis_(computing) "Jim Ellis (computing)"), originated the idea of using [Bourne shell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourne_shell "Bourne shell") scripts to transfer news and messages on a serial line [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") connection with nearby [University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill "University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill"). Following public release of the software in 1980, the mesh of UUCP hosts forwarding on the Usenet news rapidly expanded. UUCPnet, as it would later be named, also created gateways and links between [FidoNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FidoNet "FidoNet") and dial-up BBS hosts. UUCP networks spread quickly due to the lower costs involved, ability to use existing leased lines, [X.25](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.25 "X.25") links or even [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET") connections, and the lack of strict use policies compared to later networks like [CSNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSNET "CSNET") and [BITNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BITNET "BITNET"). All connects were local. By 1981 the number of UUCP hosts had grown to 550, nearly doubling to 940 in 1984.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-103)

[Sublink Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sublink_Network "Sublink Network"), operating since 1987 and officially founded in Italy in 1989, based its interconnectivity upon UUCP to redistribute mail and news groups messages throughout its Italian nodes (about 100 at the time) owned both by private individuals and small companies. Sublink Network evolved into one of the first examples of Internet technology coming into use through popular diffusion.

## 1973–1989: Merging the networks and creating the Internet

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Internet_map_in_February_82.png/250px-Internet_map_in_February_82.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_map_in_February_82.png)

Map of the [TCP/IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TCP/IP "TCP/IP") test network in February 1982

### TCP/IP

Main article: [Internet protocol suite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite "Internet protocol suite")

Further information: [Transmission Control Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol "Transmission Control Protocol") and [Internet Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Protocol "Internet Protocol")

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/First_Internet_Demonstration%2C_1977.jpg/250px-First_Internet_Demonstration%2C_1977.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:First_Internet_Demonstration,_1977.jpg)

First Internet demonstration, linking the [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET"), [PRNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PRNET "PRNET"), and [SATNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SATNET "SATNET") on November 22, 1977

With so many different networking methods seeking interconnection, a method was needed to unify them. [Louis Pouzin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Pouzin "Louis Pouzin") initiated the [CYCLADES](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CYCLADES "CYCLADES") project in 1972,[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:21-104) building on the work of [Donald Davies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Davies "Donald Davies") and the ARPANET.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey8.3-105) An [International Network Working Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Network_Working_Group "International Network Working Group") formed in 1972; active members included [Vint Cerf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vint_Cerf "Vint Cerf") from [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University"), Alex McKenzie from [BBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolt_Beranek_%26_Newman "Bolt Beranek & Newman"), Donald Davies and [Roger Scantlebury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Scantlebury "Roger Scantlebury") from [NPL](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory_(United_Kingdom) "National Physical Laboratory (United Kingdom)"), and Louis Pouzin and [Hubert Zimmermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubert_Zimmermann "Hubert Zimmermann") from [IRIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Institute_for_Research_in_Computer_Science_and_Automation "French Institute for Research in Computer Science and Automation").[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-106)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ieee201703-107)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-108) Pouzin coined the term _[catenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catenet "Catenet")_ for concatenated network. [Bob Metcalfe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Metcalfe "Robert Metcalfe") at [Xerox PARC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARC_(company) "PARC (company)") outlined the idea of [Ethernet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethernet "Ethernet") and [PARC Universal Packet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARC_Universal_Packet "PARC Universal Packet") (PUP) for [internetworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internetworking "Internetworking"). [Bob Kahn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_E._Kahn "Robert E. Kahn"), now at [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA"), recruited Vint Cerf to work with him on the problem. By 1973, these groups had worked out a fundamental reformulation, in which the differences between network protocols were hidden by using a common [internetworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internetworking "Internetworking") protocol. Instead of the network being responsible for reliability, as in the ARPANET, the hosts became responsible.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:3-2)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:5-109)

Cerf and Kahn published their ideas in May 1974,[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:7-91) which incorporated concepts implemented by Louis Pouzin and Hubert Zimmermann in the CYCLADES network.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Green_2010_31-89)[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-110) The specification of the resulting protocol, the [Transmission Control Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_Control_Program "Transmission Control Program"), was published as [RFC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RFC_(identifier) "RFC (identifier)")[675](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc675) by the Network Working Group in December 1974.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-111) It contains the first attested use of the term _internet_, as a shorthand for internetwork. This software was monolithic in design using two [simplex communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simplex_communication "Simplex communication") channels for each user session.

With the role of the network reduced to a core of functionality, it became possible to exchange traffic with other networks independently from their detailed characteristics, thereby solving the fundamental problems of internetworking. DARPA agreed to fund the development of prototype software, work on which was documented in the [Internet Experiment Notes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Experiment_Note "Internet Experiment Note"). Testing began in 1975 through concurrent implementations at Stanford, BBN and [University College London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_College_London "University College London") (UCL).[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:4-3) After several years of work, the first demonstration of a gateway between the [Packet Radio network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PRNET "PRNET") (PRNET) in the SF Bay area and the ARPANET was conducted by the [Stanford Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International "SRI International"). On November 22, 1977, a three network demonstration was conducted including the ARPANET, the SRI's [Packet Radio Van](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Packet_Radio_Van "Packet Radio Van") on the Packet Radio Network and the [Atlantic Packet Satellite Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Packet_Satellite_Network "Atlantic Packet Satellite Network") (SATNET) including a node at UCL.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-112)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-113)

The software was redesigned as a modular protocol stack, using full-duplex channels; between 1976 and 1977, [Yogen Dalal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yogen_Dalal "Yogen Dalal"), John Shoch and Robert Metcalfe among others, proposed separating TCP's [routing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Routing "Routing") and transmission control functions into two discrete layers,[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Panzaris-114)[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey-Dalal-115)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-116) which led to the splitting of the Transmission Control Program into the [Transmission Control Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol "Transmission Control Protocol") (TCP) and the [Internet Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Protocol "Internet Protocol") (IP) in version 3 in 1978.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Pelkey-Dalal-115)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:0-117)[Version 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPv4 "IPv4") was described in [IETF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IETF "IETF") publication RFC 791 (September 1981), 792 and 793. It was installed on [SATNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SATNET "SATNET") in 1982 and the ARPANET in January 1983 after the DoD made it standard for all military computer networking.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-118)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-119) This resulted in a networking model that became known informally as TCP/IP. It was also referred to as the Department of Defense (DoD) model or DARPA model.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-120) Cerf credits several of his graduate students with important work on the design and testing (see [List of Internet pioneers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Internet_pioneers#Birth_of_the_Internet_plaque "List of Internet pioneers")).[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Smithsonian-1990a-121) DARPA sponsored or encouraged the [development of TCP/IP implementations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite#Adoption "Internet protocol suite") for many operating systems.

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/IPv4_address_structure_and_writing_systems-en.svg/330px-IPv4_address_structure_and_writing_systems-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:IPv4_address_structure_and_writing_systems-en.svg)
