Decomposition of the quad-dotted IPv4 address representation to its [binary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_numeral_system "Binary numeral system") value

### From ARPANET to NSFNET

Main article: [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET")

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/InetCirca85.jpg/330px-InetCirca85.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InetCirca85.jpg)

[BBN Technologies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBN_Technologies "BBN Technologies") TCP/IP Internet map of early 1986

After the ARPANET had been up and running for several years, ARPA looked for another agency to hand off the network to; ARPA's primary mission was funding cutting-edge research and development, not running a communications utility. In July 1975, the network was turned over to the [Defense Communications Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Defense_Communications_Agency "Defense Communications Agency"), also part of the [Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense"). In 1983, the [U.S. military](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._military "U.S. military") portion of the ARPANET was broken off as a separate network, the [MILNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MILNET "MILNET"). MILNET subsequently became the unclassified but military-only [NIPRNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NIPRNET "NIPRNET"), in parallel with the SECRET-level [SIPRNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SIPRNET "SIPRNET") and [JWICS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JWICS "JWICS") for TOP SECRET and above. NIPRNET does have controlled security gateways to the public Internet.

The networks based on the ARPANET were government funded and therefore restricted to noncommercial uses such as research; unrelated commercial use was strictly forbidden.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-122) This initially restricted connections to military sites and universities. During the 1980s, the connections expanded to more educational institutions, and a growing number of companies such as [Digital Equipment Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation "Digital Equipment Corporation") and [Hewlett-Packard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hewlett-Packard "Hewlett-Packard"), which were participating in research projects or providing services to those who were. Data transmission speeds depended upon the type of connection, the slowest being analog telephone lines and the fastest using optical networking technology.

Several other branches of the [U.S. government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States "Federal government of the United States"), the [National Aeronautics and Space Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration "National Aeronautics and Space Administration") (NASA), the [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation") (NSF), and the [Department of Energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy "United States Department of Energy") (DOE) became heavily involved in Internet research and started development of a successor to ARPANET. In the mid-1980s, all three of these branches developed the first Wide Area Networks based on TCP/IP. NASA developed the [NASA Science Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NASA_Science_Network&action=edit&redlink=1 "NASA Science Network (page does not exist)"), NSF developed [CSNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSNET "CSNET") and DOE evolved the [Energy Sciences Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy_Sciences_Network "Energy Sciences Network") or ESNet.

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NSFNET-backbone-T3.png/330px-NSFNET-backbone-T3.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NSFNET-backbone-T3.png)

T3 NSFNET Backbone, c. 1992

NASA developed the TCP/IP based NASA Science Network (NSN) in the mid-1980s, connecting space scientists to data and information stored anywhere in the world. In 1989, the [DECnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DECnet "DECnet")-based Space Physics Analysis Network (SPAN) and the TCP/IP-based NASA Science Network (NSN) were brought together at NASA Ames Research Center creating the first multiprotocol wide area network called the NASA Science Internet, or NSI. NSI was established to provide a totally integrated communications infrastructure to the NASA scientific community for the advancement of earth, space and life sciences. As a high-speed, multiprotocol, international network, NSI provided connectivity to over 20,000 scientists across all seven continents.

In 1981, NSF supported the development of the [Computer Science Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSNET "CSNET") (CSNET). CSNET connected with ARPANET using TCP/IP, and ran TCP/IP over [X.25](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X.25 "X.25"), but it also supported departments without sophisticated network connections, using automated dial-up mail exchange. CSNET played a central role in popularizing the Internet outside the ARPANET.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23)

In 1986, the NSF created [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET"), a 56 kbit/s [backbone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_backbone "Internet backbone") to support the NSF-sponsored [supercomputing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercomputer "Supercomputer") centers. The NSFNET also provided support for the creation of regional research and education networks in the United States, and for the connection of university and college campus networks to the regional networks.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-123) The use of NSFNET and the regional networks was not limited to supercomputer users and the 56 kbit/s network quickly became overloaded. NSFNET was upgraded to 1.5 Mbit/s in 1988 under a cooperative agreement with the [Merit Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merit_Network "Merit Network") in partnership with [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM"), [MCI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MCI_Communications "MCI Communications"), and the [State of Michigan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_Michigan "State of Michigan"). The existence of NSFNET and the creation of [Federal Internet Exchanges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Internet_Exchange "Federal Internet Exchange") (FIXes) allowed the ARPANET to be decommissioned in 1990.

NSFNET was expanded and upgraded to dedicated fiber, optical lasers and optical amplifier systems capable of delivering T3 start up speeds or 45 Mbit/s in 1991. However, the T3 transition by MCI took longer than expected, allowing Sprint to establish a coast-to-coast long-distance commercial Internet service. When NSFNET was decommissioned in 1995, its optical networking backbones were handed off to several commercial Internet service providers, including MCI, [PSI Net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSINet "PSINet") and Sprint.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-124) As a result, when the handoff was complete, Sprint and its Washington DC Network Access Points began to carry Internet traffic, and by 1996, Sprint was the world's largest carrier of Internet traffic.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-125)

The research and academic community continues to develop and use advanced networks such as [Internet2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet2 "Internet2") in the United States and [JANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JANET "JANET") in the United Kingdom.

### Transition towards the Internet

The term "internet" was reflected in the first RFC published on the TCP protocol (RFC 675:[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-126) Internet Transmission Control Program, December 1974) as a short form of _internetworking_, when the two terms were used interchangeably. In general, an internet was a collection of networks linked by a common protocol. In the time period when the ARPANET was connected to the newly formed [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") project in the late 1980s, the term was used as the name of the network, Internet, being the large and global TCP/IP network.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-127)

Opening the Internet and the fiber optic backbone to corporate and consumers increased demand for network capacity. The expense and delay of laying new fiber led providers to test a fiber bandwidth expansion alternative that had been pioneered in the late 1970s by [Optelecom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optelecom "Optelecom") using "interactions between light and matter, such as lasers and optical devices used for [optical amplification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_amplifier "Optical amplifier") and wave mixing".[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-128) This technology became known as [wave division multiplexing (WDM)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength-division_multiplexing "Wavelength-division multiplexing"). Bell Labs deployed a 4-channel WDM system in 1995.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Winzer_Neilson_Chraplyvy_2018_p._24190-129) To develop a mass capacity (dense) WDM system, [Optelecom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optelecom "Optelecom") and its former head of Light Systems Research, [David R. Huber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_R._Huber "David R. Huber") formed a new venture, [Ciena Corp.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ciena_Corp. "Ciena Corp."), that deployed the world's first dense WDM system on the Sprint fiber network in June 1996.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Winzer_Neilson_Chraplyvy_2018_p._24190-129) This was referred to as the real start of optical networking.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-130)

As interest in networking grew by needs of collaboration, exchange of data, and access of remote computing resources, the Internet technologies spread throughout the rest of the world. The hardware-agnostic approach in TCP/IP supported the use of existing network infrastructure, such as the [International Packet Switched Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Packet_Switched_Service "International Packet Switched Service") (IPSS) X.25 network, to carry Internet traffic.

Many sites unable to link directly to the Internet created simple gateways for the transfer of electronic mail, the most important application of the time. Sites with only intermittent connections used [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") or [FidoNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FidoNet "FidoNet") and relied on the gateways between these networks and the Internet. Some gateway services went beyond simple mail peering, such as allowing access to [File Transfer Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol "File Transfer Protocol") (FTP) sites via UUCP or mail.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-131)

Finally, routing technologies were developed for the Internet to remove the remaining centralized routing aspects. The [Exterior Gateway Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exterior_Gateway_Protocol "Exterior Gateway Protocol") (EGP) was replaced by a new protocol, the [Border Gateway Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border_Gateway_Protocol "Border Gateway Protocol") (BGP). This provided a meshed topology for the Internet and reduced the centric architecture which ARPANET had emphasized. In 1994, [Classless Inter-Domain Routing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classless_Inter-Domain_Routing "Classless Inter-Domain Routing") (CIDR) was introduced to support better conservation of address space which allowed use of [route aggregation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernet "Supernet") to decrease the size of [routing tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Routing_table "Routing table").[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-132)

### Optical networking

The [MOS transistor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MOS_transistor "MOS transistor") underpinned the rapid growth of telecommunication bandwidth over the second half of the 20th century.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Jindal-133) To address the need for transmission capacity beyond that provided by [radio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio "Radio"), [satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite "Satellite") and analog copper telephone lines, engineers developed [optical communications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_communication "Optical communication") systems based on [fiber optic cables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber-optic_cable "Fiber-optic cable") powered by [lasers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laser "Laser") and [optical amplifier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_amplifier "Optical amplifier") techniques.

The concept of lasing arose from a 1917 paper by [Albert Einstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein "Albert Einstein"), "On the Quantum Theory of Radiation". Einstein expanded upon a conversation with [Max Planck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Max_Planck "Max Planck") on how [atoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atom "Atom") absorb and emit [light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light "Light"), part of a thought process that, with input from [Erwin Schrödinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger "Erwin Schrödinger"), [Werner Heisenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg "Werner Heisenberg") and others, gave rise to [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics"). Specifically, in his quantum theory, Einstein mathematically determined that light could be generated not only by [spontaneous emission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_emission "Spontaneous emission"), such as the light emitted by an [incandescent light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb "Incandescent light bulb") or the Sun, but also by [stimulated emission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stimulated_emission "Stimulated emission").

Forty years later, on November 13, 1957, [Columbia University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbia_University "Columbia University") physics student [Gordon Gould](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gordon_Gould "Gordon Gould") first realized how to make light by stimulated emission through a process of [optical amplification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_amplification "Optical amplification"). He coined the term LASER for this technology—Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-134) Using Gould's light amplification method (patented as "Optically Pumped Laser Amplifier"),[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-135)[Theodore Maiman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore_Maiman "Theodore Maiman") made the first working laser on May 16, 1960.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-136)

Gould co-founded [Optelecom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optelecom "Optelecom") in 1973 to commercialize his inventions in optical fiber telecommunications,[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-137) just as [Corning Glass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corning_Glass "Corning Glass") was producing the first commercial fiber optic cable in small quantities. Optelecom configured its own fiber lasers and optical amplifiers into the first commercial optical communication systems which it delivered to [Chevron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chevron_Corporation "Chevron Corporation") and the US Army Missile Defense.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-138) Three years later, [GTE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GTE "GTE") deployed the first optical telephone system in 1977 in Long Beach, California.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-139) By the early 1980s, optical networks powered by lasers, [LED](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LED "LED") and optical amplifier equipment supplied by [Bell Labs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_Labs "Bell Labs"), [NTT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NTT_Docomo "NTT Docomo") and [Perelli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pirelli "Pirelli")[_[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please\_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_] were used by select universities and long-distance telephone providers.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

### TCP/IP goes global (1980s)

#### SATNET, CERN and the European Internet

See also: [Protocol Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protocol_Wars "Protocol Wars")

In 1982, Norway ([NORSAR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NORSAR "NORSAR") and [NDRE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norwegian_Defence_Research_Establishment "Norwegian Defence Research Establishment")) and [Peter Kirstein's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_T._Kirstein "Peter T. Kirstein") research group at [University College London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_College_London "University College London") (UCL) left the ARPANET and reconnected using TCP/IP over [SATNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SATNET "SATNET").[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:5-109)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-140) There were 40 [British research groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_in_the_United_Kingdom#Early_years "Internet in the United Kingdom") using UCL's link to ARPANET in 1975;[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:9-80) by 1984 there was a user population of about 150 people on both sides of the Atlantic.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-141)

Between 1984 and 1988, [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN") began installation and operation of TCP/IP to interconnect its major internal computer systems, workstations, PCs, and an accelerator control system. CERN continued to operate a limited self-developed system (CERNET) internally and several incompatible (typically proprietary) network protocols externally. There was considerable resistance in Europe towards more widespread use of TCP/IP, and the CERN TCP/IP intranets remained isolated from the Internet until 1989, when a transatlantic connection to Cornell University was established.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:6-142)[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-143)[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-nsf-144)

The [Computer Science Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSNET "CSNET") (CSNET) began operation in 1981 to provide networking connections to institutions that could not connect directly to ARPANET. Its first international connection was to Israel in 1984. Soon after, connections were established to computer science departments in Canada, France, and Germany.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23)

In 1988, the first international connections to [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation_Network "National Science Foundation Network") was established by France's [INRIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Institute_for_Research_in_Computer_Science_and_Automation "French Institute for Research in Computer Science and Automation"),[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:02-145)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-146) and [Piet Beertema](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piet_Beertema "Piet Beertema") at the [Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica "Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica") (CWI) in the Netherlands.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-147) Daniel Karrenberg, from CWI, visited [Ben Segal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben_Segal_(computer_scientist) "Ben Segal (computer scientist)"), CERN's TCP/IP coordinator, looking for advice about the transition of [EUnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EUnet "EUnet"), the European side of the UUCP Usenet network (much of which ran over X.25 links), over to TCP/IP. The previous year, Segal had met with [Len Bosack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Len_Bosack "Len Bosack") from the then still small company [Cisco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cisco_Systems "Cisco Systems") about purchasing some TCP/IP routers for CERN, and Segal was able to give Karrenberg advice and forward him on to Cisco for the appropriate hardware. This expanded the European portion of the Internet across the existing UUCP networks. The [NORDUnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NORDUnet "NORDUnet") connection to NSFNET was in place soon after, providing open access for university students in Denmark, Finland, Iceland, Norway, and Sweden.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-148)

In January 1989, CERN opened its first external TCP/IP connections.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-149) This coincided with the creation of Réseaux IP Européens ([RIPE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RIPE "RIPE")), initially a group of IP network administrators who met regularly to carry out coordination work together. Later, in 1992, RIPE was formally registered as a [cooperative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative "Cooperative") in Amsterdam.

The United Kingdom's [national research and education network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_research_and_education_network "National research and education network") (NREN), [JANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JANET "JANET"), began operation in 1984 using the UK's [Coloured Book protocols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coloured_Book_protocols "Coloured Book protocols") and connected to NSFNET in 1989. In 1991, JANET adopted Internet Protocol on the existing network.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-150)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-151) The same year, Dai Davies introduced Internet technology into the pan-European NREN, [EuropaNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DANTE "DANTE"), which was built on the X.25 protocol.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-152)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-153) The [European Academic and Research Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Academic_and_Research_Network "European Academic and Research Network") (EARN) and [RARE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TERENA "TERENA") adopted IP around the same time, and the European Internet backbone [EBONE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EBONE "EBONE") became operational in 1992.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:6-142)

Nonetheless, for a period in the late 1980s and early 1990s, engineers, organizations and nations were [polarized over the issue of which standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protocol_Wars "Protocol Wars"), the [OSI model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OSI_model "OSI model") or the Internet protocol suite would result in the best and most robust computer networks.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ieee201703-107)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-154)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-155)

#### The link to the Pacific

Japan, which had built the UUCP-based network [JUNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JUNET "JUNET") in 1984, connected to CSNET,[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23) and later to NSFNET in 1989, marking the spread of the Internet to Asia.

South Korea set up a two-node domestic TCP/IP network in 1982, the System Development Network (SDN), adding a third node the following year. SDN was connected to the rest of the world in August 1983 using UUCP (Unix-to-Unix-Copy); connected to CSNET in December 1984;[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23) and formally connected to the NSFNET in 1990.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-156)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-157)[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-158)

In Australia, ad hoc networking to ARPA and in-between Australian universities formed in the late 1980s, based on various technologies such as X.25, [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP")Net, and via a CSNET.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:10-23) These were limited in their connection to the global networks, due to the cost of making individual international UUCP dial-up or X.25 connections. In 1989, Australian universities joined the push towards using IP protocols to unify their networking infrastructures. [AARNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AARNet "AARNet") was formed in 1989 by the [Australian Vice-Chancellors' Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Vice-Chancellors%27_Committee "Australian Vice-Chancellors' Committee") and provided a dedicated IP based network for Australia.

New Zealand adopted the UK's [Coloured Book protocols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coloured_Book_protocols "Coloured Book protocols") as an interim standard and established its first international IP connection to the U.S. in 1989.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-159)

#### A "digital divide" emerges

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg/500px-InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg)

Main articles: [Global digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_digital_divide "Global digital divide") and [Digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_divide "Digital divide")

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg/500px-FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:FixedBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap.svg)

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap_2013.svg/500px-MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap_2013.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MobileBroadbandInternetPenetrationWorldMap_2013.svg)

**[Mobile broadband Internet subscriptions in 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_broadband_Internet_subscriptions "List of countries by number of broadband Internet subscriptions")

Source: [International Telecommunication Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union "International Telecommunication Union").[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-MobleBroadbandITUDynamic2012-162)

While developed countries with technological infrastructures were joining the Internet, [developing countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developing_country "Developing country") began to experience a [digital divide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_digital_divide "Global digital divide") separating them from the Internet. On an essentially continental basis, they built organizations for Internet resource administration and to share operational experience, which enabled more transmission facilities to be put into place.

#### Africa

At the beginning of the 1990s, African countries relied upon X.25 [IPSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Packet_Switched_Service "International Packet Switched Service") and 2400 baud modem UUCP links for international and internetwork computer communications.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-163)

In August 1995, InfoMail Uganda, Ltd., a privately held firm in Kampala now known as InfoCom, and NSN Network Services of Avon, Colorado, sold in 1997 and now known as Clear Channel Satellite, established Africa's first native TCP/IP high-speed satellite Internet services. The data connection was originally carried by a C-Band RSCC Russian satellite which connected InfoMail's Kampala offices directly to NSN's MAE-West point of presence using a private network from NSN's leased ground station in New Jersey. InfoCom's first satellite connection was just 64 kbit/s, serving a Sun host computer and twelve US Robotics dial-up modems.

In 1996, a [USAID](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USAID "USAID") funded project, the [Leland Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leland_Initiative "Leland Initiative"), started work on developing full Internet connectivity for the continent. [Guinea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea "Guinea"), Mozambique, [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar") and [Rwanda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rwanda "Rwanda") gained [satellite earth stations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite_earth_station "Satellite earth station") in 1997, followed by [Ivory Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivory_Coast "Ivory Coast") and [Benin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benin "Benin") in 1998.

Africa is building an Internet infrastructure. [AFRINIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AFRINIC "AFRINIC"), headquartered in [Mauritius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mauritius "Mauritius"), manages IP address allocation for the continent. As with other Internet regions, there is an operational forum, the Internet Community of Operational Networking Specialists.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-164)

There are many programs to provide high-performance transmission plant, and the western and southern coasts have undersea optical cable. High-speed cables join North Africa and the Horn of Africa to intercontinental cable systems. Undersea cable development is slower for East Africa; the original joint effort between [New Partnership for Africa's Development (NEPAD)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NEPAD "NEPAD") and the East Africa Submarine System (Eassy) has broken off and may become two efforts.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-165)

#### Asia and Oceania

The [Asia Pacific Network Information Centre (APNIC)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APNIC "APNIC"), headquartered in Australia, manages IP address allocation for the continent. APNIC sponsors an operational forum, the Asia-Pacific Regional Internet Conference on Operational Technologies (APRICOT).[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-166)

In South Korea, VDSL, a last mile technology developed in the 1990s by NextLevel Communications, connected corporate and consumer copper-based telephone lines to the Internet.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-167)

The People's Republic of China established its first TCP/IP college network, [Tsinghua University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsinghua_University "Tsinghua University")'s TUNET in 1991. The PRC went on to make its first global Internet connection in 1994, between the Beijing Electro-Spectrometer Collaboration and [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University")'s Linear Accelerator Center. However, China went on to implement its own digital divide by implementing a country-wide [content filter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China "Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China").[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-168)

Japan hosted the annual meeting of the [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society"), INET'92, in [Kobe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kobe "Kobe"). Singapore developed [TECHNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TechNet_(computer_network) "TechNet (computer network)") in 1990, and Thailand gained a global Internet connection between Chulalongkorn University and UUNET in 1992.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-169)

#### Latin America

As with the other regions, [the Latin American and Caribbean Internet Addresses Registry (LACNIC)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LACNIC "LACNIC") manages the IP address space and other resources for its area. LACNIC, headquartered in Uruguay, operates DNS root, reverse DNS, and other key services.

## 1989–2004: Rise of the global Internet, Web 1.0

Main articles: [History of the World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_World_Wide_Web "History of the World Wide Web") and [Information Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_Age "Information Age")

### Development

Initially, as with its predecessor networks, the system that would evolve into the Internet was primarily for government and government body use. Although commercial use was forbidden, the exact definition of commercial use was unclear and subjective. [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP")Net and the X.25 [IPSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Packet_Switched_Service "International Packet Switched Service") had no such restrictions, which would eventually see the official barring of UUCPNet use of [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET") and [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") connections.

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg/500px-Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Internet_Hosts_Count_log.svg)

**Number of Internet hosts worldwide: 1969–2019**

Source: [Internet Systems Consortium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Systems_Consortium "Internet Systems Consortium").[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-170)

As a result, during the late 1980s, the first [Internet service provider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") (ISP) companies were formed. Companies like [PSINet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSINet "PSINet"), [UUNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUNET "UUNET"), [Netcom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netcom_(USA) "Netcom (USA)"), and [Portal Software](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portal_Software "Portal Software") were formed to provide service to the regional research networks and provide alternate network access, UUCP-based email and [Usenet News](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet") to the public. In 1989, [MCI Mail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MCI_Mail "MCI Mail") became the first commercial email provider to get an experimental gateway to the Internet.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-171) The first commercial dialup ISP in the United States was [The World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_World_(internet_service_provider) "The World (internet service provider)"), which opened in 1989.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-172)

In 1992, the U.S. Congress passed the Scientific and Advanced-Technology Act, [42 U.S.C.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code "Title 42 of the United States Code")[§1862(g)](https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/1862(g)), which allowed NSF to support access by the research and education communities to computer networks which were not used exclusively for research and education purposes, thus permitting NSFNET to interconnect with commercial networks.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ogc-00-33r_p6-173)[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NSFAUPNote1-174) This caused controversy within the research and education community, who were concerned commercial use of the network might lead to an Internet that was less responsive to their needs, and within the community of commercial network providers, who felt that government subsidies were giving an unfair advantage to some organizations.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NSFNETHearing1992-175)

By 1990, ARPANET's goals had been fulfilled and new networking technologies exceeded the original scope and the project came to a close. New network service providers including [PSINet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSINet "PSINet"), [Alternet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUNET#Early_existence "UUNET"), CERFNet, ANS CO+RE, and many others were offering network access to commercial customers. [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") was no longer the de facto backbone and exchange point of the Internet. The [Commercial Internet eXchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Internet_eXchange "Commercial Internet eXchange") (CIX), [Metropolitan Area Exchanges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MAE-East "MAE-East") (MAEs), and later [Network Access Points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Access_Point "Network Access Point") (NAPs) were becoming the primary interconnections between many networks. The final restrictions on carrying commercial traffic ended on April 30, 1995, when the National Science Foundation ended its sponsorship of the NSFNET Backbone Service.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-176)[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-177) NSF provided initial support for the NAPs and interim support to help the regional research and education networks transition to commercial ISPs. NSF also sponsored the [very high speed Backbone Network Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VBNS "VBNS") (vBNS) which continued to provide support for the supercomputing centers and research and education in the United States.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-178)

An event held on 11 January 1994, _[The Superhighway Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Superhighway\_Summit "The Superhighway Summit")_ at [UCLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UCLA "UCLA")'s Royce Hall, was the "first public conference bringing together all of the major industry, government and academic leaders in the field [and] also began the national dialogue about the _[Information Superhighway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information\_Superhighway "Information Superhighway")_ and its implications".[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-179)

### Internet use in wider society

The invention of the [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web") by [Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee "Tim Berners-Lee") at [CERN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CERN "CERN"), as an application on the Internet,[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-180) brought many social and commercial uses to what was, at the time, a network of networks for academic and research institutions.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-181)[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-182) The Web opened to the public in 1991 and began to enter general use in 1993–4, when [websites for everyday use](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_websites_founded_before_1995 "List of websites founded before 1995") started to become available.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-183)

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Russia-Denmark_1993-envelope.jpg/250px-Russia-Denmark_1993-envelope.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Russia-Denmark_1993-envelope.jpg)

[Stamped envelope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stamped_envelope "Stamped envelope") of [Russian Post](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Post "Russian Post") issued in 1993 with stamp and graphics dedicated to first Russian [underwater digital optic cable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable#Optical_telecommunications_cables "Submarine communications cable") laid in 1993 by [Rostelecom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rostelecom "Rostelecom") from [Kingisepp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingisepp "Kingisepp") to [Copenhagen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copenhagen "Copenhagen")

During the first decade or so of the public Internet, the immense changes it would eventually enable in the 2000s were still nascent. In terms of providing context for this period, [mobile cellular devices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellphone "Cellphone") ("smartphones" and other cellular devices) which today provide near-universal access, were used for business and not a routine household item owned by parents and children worldwide. [Social media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_media "Social media") in the modern sense had yet to come into existence, laptops were bulky and most households did not have computers. Data rates were slow and most people lacked means to video or digitize video; media storage was transitioning slowly from [analog tape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analog_tape "Analog tape") to [digital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_data "Digital data")[optical discs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_disc "Optical disc") ([DVD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DVD "DVD") and to an extent still, [floppy disc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floppy_disc "Floppy disc") to [CD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CD "CD")). Enabling technologies used from the early 2000s such as [PHP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PHP "PHP"), modern [JavaScript](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JavaScript "JavaScript") and [Java](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_(programming_language) "Java (programming language)"), technologies such as [AJAX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AJAX "AJAX"), [HTML 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTML_4 "HTML 4") (and its emphasis on [CSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CSS "CSS")), and various [software frameworks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_framework "Software framework"), which enabled and simplified speed of web development, largely awaited invention and their eventual widespread adoption.

The Internet was widely used for [mailing lists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mailing_list "Mailing list"), [emails](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email"), [creating and distributing maps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_GIS "Internet GIS") with tools like [MapQuest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MapQuest "MapQuest"), [e-commerce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-commerce "E-commerce") and early popular [online shopping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_shopping "Online shopping") ([Amazon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon.com "Amazon.com") and [eBay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EBay "EBay") for example), [online forums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_forum "Online forum") and [bulletin boards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board "Bulletin board"), and personal websites and [blogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blog "Blog"), and use was growing rapidly, but by more modern standards, the systems used were static and lacked widespread social engagement. It awaited a number of events in the early 2000s to change from a communications technology to gradually develop into a key part of global society's infrastructure.

Typical design elements of these "Web 1.0" era websites included:[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-184) Static pages instead of [dynamic HTML](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_HTML "Dynamic HTML");[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-185) content served from [filesystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filesystem "Filesystem") instead of [relational databases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relational_database "Relational database"); pages built using [Server Side Includes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Server_Side_Includes "Server Side Includes") or [CGI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface "Common Gateway Interface") instead of a [web application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application") written in a [dynamic programming language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language "Dynamic programming language"); [HTML 3.2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTML_3.2 "HTML 3.2")-era structures such as [frames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Framing_(World_Wide_Web) "Framing (World Wide Web)") and tables to create page layouts; online [guestbooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guestbook "Guestbook"); overuse of [GIF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GIF "GIF") buttons and similar small graphics promoting particular items;[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-186) and HTML forms sent via [email](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email"). (Support for [server side scripting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Server_side_scripting "Server side scripting") was rare on [shared servers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_server "Shared server") so the usual feedback mechanism was via email, using [mailto forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mailto "Mailto") and their [email program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email_client "Email client").[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-187)

During the period 1997 to 2001, the first [speculative investment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speculative_investment "Speculative investment")[bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Investment_bubble "Investment bubble") related to the Internet took place, in which ["dot-com" companies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_company "Dot-com company") (referring to the "[.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.com ".com")" [top level domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top_level_domain "Top level domain") used by businesses) were propelled to exceedingly high valuations as investors rapidly stoked [stock values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock_value "Stock value"), followed by a [market crash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Market_crash "Market crash"); the first [dot-com bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_bubble "Dot-com bubble"). However this only temporarily slowed enthusiasm and growth, which quickly recovered and continued to grow.

The [history of the World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_World_Wide_Web "History of the World Wide Web") up to around 2004 was retrospectively named and described by some as "Web 1.0".[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-188)

### IPv6

In the final stage of [IPv4 address exhaustion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPv4_address_exhaustion "IPv4 address exhaustion"), the last IPv4 address block was assigned in January 2011 at the level of the regional Internet registries.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ins-189) IPv4 uses 32-[bit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bit "Bit") addresses which limits the [address space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Address_space "Address space") to 2 32 addresses, i.e. 4 294 967 296 addresses.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-:0-117) IPv4 is in the process of replacement by [IPv6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPv6 "IPv6"), its successor, which uses 128-bit addresses, providing 2 128 addresses, i.e. 340 282 366 920 938 463 463 374 607 431 768 211 456,[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-190) a vastly increased address space. The shift to IPv6 is expected to take a long time to complete.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ins-189)

## 2004–present: Web 2.0, global ubiquity, social media

Main articles: [Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0 "Web 2.0") and [History of the World Wide Web §Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_World_Wide_Web#Web_2.0 "History of the World Wide Web")

The rapid technical advances that would propel the Internet into its place as a social system, which has completely transformed the way humans interact with each other, took place during a relatively short period from around 2005 to 2010, coinciding with the point in time in which [IoT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_of_things "Internet of things") devices surpassed the number of humans alive at some point in the late 2000s. They included:

*   The call to "[Web 2.0](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0 "Web 2.0")" in 2004 (first suggested in 1999).
*   Accelerating adoption and commoditization among households of, and familiarity with, the necessary hardware (such as computers).
*   Accelerating storage technology and data access speeds – [hard drives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hard_drive "Hard drive") emerged, took over from far smaller, slower [floppy discs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floppy_disc "Floppy disc"), and grew from [megabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megabyte "Megabyte") to [gigabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gigabyte "Gigabyte") (and by around 2010, [terabytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terabyte "Terabyte")), [RAM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random-access_memory "Random-access memory") from hundreds of [kilobytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilobyte "Kilobyte") to gigabytes as typical amounts on a system, and [Ethernet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethernet "Ethernet"), the enabling technology for TCP/IP, moved from common speeds of kilobits to tens of megabits per second, to gigabits per second.
*   High speed Internet and wider coverage of data connections, at lower prices, allowing larger traffic rates, more reliable simpler traffic, and traffic from more locations.
*   The public's accelerating perception of the potential of computers to create new means and approaches to communication, the emergence of social media and websites such as [Twitter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twitter "Twitter") and [Facebook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Facebook "Facebook") to their later prominence, and global collaborations such as [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia "Wikipedia") (which existed before but gained prominence as a result).
*   The mobile device revolution, particularly with smartphones and tablet computers becoming widespread, which began to provide easy access to the Internet to much of human society of all ages, in their daily lives, and allowed them to share, discuss, and continually update, inquire, and respond.
*   [Non-volatile RAM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-volatile_RAM "Non-volatile RAM") rapidly grew in size and reliability, and decreased in price, becoming a commodity capable of enabling high levels of computing activity on these small handheld devices as well as [solid-state drives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid-state_drive "Solid-state drive") (SSD).
*   An emphasis on power efficient processor and device design, rather than purely high processing power; one of the beneficiaries of this was [Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arm_(company) "Arm (company)"), a British company which had focused since the 1980s on powerful but low cost simple microprocessors. The [ARM architecture family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARM_architecture_family "ARM architecture family") rapidly gained dominance in the market for mobile and embedded devices.

### Web 2.0

The term "Web 2.0" describes [websites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Website "Website") that emphasize [user-generated content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User-generated_content "User-generated content") (including user-to-user interaction), [usability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usability "Usability"), and [interoperability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_API "Web API"). It first appeared in a January 1999 article called "Fragmented Future" written by [Darcy DiNucci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darcy_DiNucci "Darcy DiNucci"), a consultant on [electronic information design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_architecture "Information architecture"), where she wrote:[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-graham-191)[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-oreilly-192)[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-193)[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DiNucci-194)

> The Web we know now, which loads into a browser window in essentially static screenfuls, is only an embryo of the Web to come. The first glimmerings of Web 2.0 are beginning to appear, and we are just starting to see how that embryo might develop. The Web will be understood not as screenfuls of text and graphics but as a transport mechanism, the ether through which interactivity happens. It will [...] appear on your computer screen, [...] on your TV set [...] your car dashboard [...] your cell phone [...] hand-held game machines [...] maybe even your microwave oven.

The term resurfaced during 2002–2004,[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-195)[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-196)[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Knorr,_Eric_2003-197)[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-jrobb.mindplex.org-198) and gained prominence in late 2004 following presentations by [Tim O'Reilly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_O%27Reilly "Tim O'Reilly") and Dale Dougherty at the first [Web 2.0 Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0_Summit "Web 2.0 Summit"). In their opening remarks, [John Battelle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Battelle "John Battelle") and Tim O'Reilly outlined their definition of the "Web as Platform", where software applications are built upon the Web as opposed to upon the desktop. The unique aspect of this migration, they argued, is that "customers are building your business for you".[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-O'Reilly,_Tim_2004-199)[_[non-primary source needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No\_original\_research#Primary,\_secondary\_and\_tertiary\_sources "Wikipedia:No original research")_] They argued that the activities of users generating content (in the form of ideas, text, videos, or pictures) could be "harnessed" to create value.

"Web 2.0" does not refer to an update to any technical specification, but rather to cumulative changes in the way Web pages are made and used. "Web 2.0" describes an approach, in which sites focus substantially upon allowing users to interact and collaborate with each other in a [social media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_media "Social media") dialogue as creators of [user-generated content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/User-generated_content "User-generated content") in a [virtual community](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virtual_community "Virtual community"), in contrast to Web sites where people are limited to the passive viewing of [content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Content_(media_and_publishing) "Content (media and publishing)"). Examples of Web 2.0 include [social networking services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_networking_service "Social networking service"), [blogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blog "Blog"), [wikis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wiki "Wiki"), [folksonomies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folksonomy "Folksonomy"), [video sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_sharing "Video sharing") sites, [hosted services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_service "Web service"), [Web applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application"), and [mashups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mashup_(web_application_hybrid) "Mashup (web application hybrid)").[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-200)[Terry Flew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terry_Flew "Terry Flew"), in his 3rd edition of _New Media_, described what he believed to characterize the differences between Web 1.0 and Web 2.0:

> [The] move from personal websites to blogs and blog site aggregation, from publishing to participation, from web content as the outcome of large up-front investment to an ongoing and interactive process, and from content management systems to links based on tagging (folksonomy).[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-201)

This era saw several household names gain prominence through their community-oriented operation – [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube"), Twitter, Facebook, [Reddit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reddit "Reddit") and Wikipedia being some examples.

### Telephone networks convert to VoIP

Telephone systems have been slowly adopting [voice over IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voice_over_IP "Voice over IP") since 2003. Early experiments proved that voice can be converted to digital packets and sent over the Internet. The packets are collected and converted back to analog voice.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-202)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-203)[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-204)

### The mobile revolution

Main articles: [History of mobile phones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_mobile_phones "History of mobile phones"), [Mobile web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_web "Mobile web"), and [Responsive web design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Responsive_web_design "Responsive web design")

The process of change that generally coincided with Web 2.0 was itself greatly accelerated and transformed only a short time later by the increasing growth in mobile devices. This mobile revolution meant that computers in the form of smartphones became something many people used, took with them everywhere, communicated with, used for photographs and videos they instantly shared or to shop or seek information "on the move" – and used socially, as opposed to items on a desk at home or just used for work.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Location-based services, services using location and other sensor information, and [crowdsourcing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crowdsourcing "Crowdsourcing") (frequently but not always location based), became common, with posts tagged by location, or websites and services becoming location aware. Mobile-targeted websites (such as "m.example.com") became common, designed especially for the new devices used. [Netbooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netbook "Netbook"), [ultrabooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultrabook "Ultrabook"), widespread [4G](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4G "4G") and [Wi-Fi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wi-Fi "Wi-Fi"), and mobile chips capable or running at nearly the power of desktops from not many years before on far lower power usage, became enablers of this stage of Internet development, and the term "[App](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/App_(computing) "App (computing)")" (short for "Application program" or "Program") became popularized, as did the "[App store](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/App_store "App store")".

This "mobile revolution" has allowed for people to have a nearly unlimited amount of information at all times. With the ability to access the internet from cell phones came a change in the way media was consumed. Media consumption statistics show that over half of media consumption between those aged 18 and 34 were using a smartphone.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-205)

### Future Internet initiatives

In the transition to Web 2.0 in the mid-2000s, a number of [research groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research_group "Research group") emerged aiming to improve the existing infrastructure of the Internet.[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-fm-206) These future Internet groups coalesced around the idea that the current Internet and its legacy protocols were inadequate to support the exponential increase in traffic and the integration of mobile technologies.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-survey-207) A number of experimental networking schemes and protocols were developed, seeking to either build upon the existing Internet in an evolutionary approach or to supplant it entirely in a clean-slate approach.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-208) These schemes varied in scope, but usually prioritized enhanced support for mobile networking, [cloud computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_computing "Cloud computing"), [content delivery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Content_delivery "Content delivery"), and [user privacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_privacy "Internet privacy").[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-foundation-209)

The most prominent of these future Internet groups included the NSF's Future Internet Design (FIND), established in 2006, as well as its successor, the Future Internet Architecture (FIA), established in 2010.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-210)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-foundation-209) Following the NSF's lead, in 2006 Japan's [NICT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Institute_of_Information_and_Communications_Technology "National Institute of Information and Communications Technology") established the [AKARI Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AKARI_Project "AKARI Project"), while in 2008 the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") established the Evolved Internet Future for European Leadership (EIFFEL).[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-survey-207)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-foundation-209) These efforts were supported by experimental [testbeds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Testbed "Testbed"), such as the NSF's [Global Environment for Network Innovations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Environment_for_Network_Innovations "Global Environment for Network Innovations") (GENI); the EU's [Future Internet Research and Experimentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Future_Internet_Research_and_Experimentation "Future Internet Research and Experimentation") (FIRE); and NICT's JGN2plus. Each of these testbeds facilitated the large-scale validation of their networking schemes and protocols.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-survey-207) One of the few tangible results of the NSF's future Internet initiatives include the increased use of [software-defined networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software-defined_networking "Software-defined networking") as well as the proposal for [named data networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Named_data_networking "Named data networking").[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-foundation-209)[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-fm-206) Both FIRE and GENI dissolved in 2022 and 2023 respectively.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-211)[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-212)

### Networking in outer space

Main article: [Interplanetary Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interplanetary_Internet "Interplanetary Internet")

The first Internet link into [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") was established on January 22, 2010, when astronaut [T. J. Creamer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timothy_Creamer "Timothy Creamer") posted the first unassisted update to his Twitter account from the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station"), marking the extension of the Internet into space.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-213) (Astronauts at the ISS had used email and Twitter before, but these messages had been relayed to the ground through a NASA data link before being posted by a human proxy.) This personal Web access, which NASA calls the Crew Support LAN, uses the space station's high-speed [Ku band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ku_band "Ku band") microwave link. To surf the Web, astronauts can use a station laptop computer to control a desktop computer on Earth, and they can talk to their families and friends on Earth using [Voice over IP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voice_over_IP "Voice over IP") equipment.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-214)

Communication with spacecraft beyond Earth orbit has traditionally been over point-to-point links through the [Deep Space Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_Space_Network "Deep Space Network"). Each such data link must be manually scheduled and configured. In the late 1990s NASA and Google began working on a new network protocol, [delay-tolerant networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delay-tolerant_networking "Delay-tolerant networking") (DTN), which automates this process, allows networking of spaceborne transmission nodes, and takes the fact into account that spacecraft can temporarily lose contact because they move behind the Moon or planets, or because [space weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_weather "Space weather") disrupts the connection. Under such conditions, DTN retransmits data packages instead of dropping them, as the standard TCP/IP Internet Protocol does. NASA conducted the first field test of what it calls the "deep space internet" in November 2008.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-215) Testing of DTN-based communications between the International Space Station and Earth (now termed disruption-tolerant networking) has been ongoing since March 2009, and was scheduled to continue until March 2014.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-216)[_[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Dates\_and\_numbers#Chronological\_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_]

This network technology is supposed to ultimately enable missions that involve multiple spacecraft where reliable inter-vessel communication might take precedence over vessel-to-Earth downlinks. According to a February 2011 statement by Google's [Vint Cerf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vint_Cerf "Vint Cerf"), the so-called "bundle protocols" have been uploaded to NASA's [EPOXI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPOXI "EPOXI") mission spacecraft (which is in orbit around the Sun) and communication with Earth has been tested at a distance of approximately 80 light seconds.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-217)

## Internet governance

Main article: [Internet governance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_governance "Internet governance")

As a [globally distributed network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_network "Global network") of voluntarily interconnected autonomous networks, the Internet operates without a central governing body. Each constituent network chooses the technologies and protocols it deploys from the technical standards that are developed by the [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF).[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-218) However, successful interoperation of many networks requires certain parameters that must be common throughout the network. For managing such parameters, the [Internet Assigned Numbers Authority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Assigned_Numbers_Authority "Internet Assigned Numbers Authority") (IANA) oversees the allocation and assignment of various technical identifiers.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DDN_NIC-219) In addition, the [Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Corporation_for_Assigned_Names_and_Numbers "Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers") (ICANN) provides oversight and coordination for the two principal [name spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Name_space "Name space") in the Internet, the [Internet Protocol address space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IP_address "IP address") and the [Domain Name System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_Name_System "Domain Name System").

### NIC, InterNIC, IANA, and ICANN

The IANA function was originally performed by USC Information Sciences Institute (ISI), and it delegated portions of this responsibility with respect to numeric network and autonomous system identifiers to the [Network Information Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Information_Center "Network Information Center") (NIC) at [Stanford Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_Research_Institute "Stanford Research Institute") (SRI International) in [Menlo Park, California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Menlo_Park,_California "Menlo Park, California"). ISI's [Jonathan Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jonathan_Postel "Jonathan Postel") managed the IANA, served as RFC Editor and performed other key roles until his death in 1998.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-220)

As the early ARPANET grew, hosts were referred to by names, and a HOSTS.TXT file would be distributed from [SRI International](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International "SRI International") to each host on the network. As the network grew, this became cumbersome. A technical solution came in the form of the [Domain Name System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_Name_System "Domain Name System"), created by ISI's [Paul Mockapetris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Mockapetris "Paul Mockapetris") in 1983.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-221) The Defense Data Network—Network Information Center (DDN-NIC) at SRI handled all registration services, including the [top-level domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top-level_domain "Top-level domain") (TLDs) of [.mil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.mil ".mil"), [.gov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.gov ".gov"), [.edu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.edu ".edu"), [.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.org ".org"), [.net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.net ".net"), [.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.com ".com") and [.us](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/.us ".us"), [root nameserver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_nameserver "Root nameserver") administration and Internet number assignments under a [United States Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") contract.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DDN_NIC-219) In 1991, the Defense Information Systems Agency (DISA) awarded the administration and maintenance of DDN-NIC (managed by SRI up until this point) to Government Systems, Inc., who subcontracted it to the small private-sector [Network Solutions, Inc.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Solutions "Network Solutions")[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-222)[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-223)

The increasing cultural diversity of the Internet also posed administrative challenges for centralized management of the IP addresses. In October 1992, the Internet Engineering Task Force (IETF) published RFC 1366,[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-224) which described the "growth of the Internet and its increasing globalization" and set out the basis for an evolution of the IP registry process, based on a regionally distributed registry model. This document stressed the need for a single Internet number registry to exist in each geographical region of the world (which would be of "continental dimensions"). Registries would be "unbiased and widely recognized by network providers and subscribers" within their region. The RIPE Network Coordination Centre (RIPE NCC) was established as the first RIR in May 1992. The second RIR, the Asia Pacific Network Information Centre (APNIC), was established in Tokyo in 1993, as a pilot project of the Asia Pacific Networking Group.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Cisco-225)

Since at this point in history most of the growth on the Internet was coming from non-military sources, it was decided that the [Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") would no longer fund registration services outside of the .mil TLD. In 1993 the U.S. [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation"), after a competitive bidding process in 1992, created the [InterNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/InterNIC "InterNIC") to manage the allocations of addresses and management of the address databases, and awarded the contract to three organizations. Registration Services would be provided by [Network Solutions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Network_Solutions "Network Solutions"); Directory and Database Services would be provided by [AT&T](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation "AT&T Corporation"); and Information Services would be provided by [General Atomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Atomics "General Atomics").[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-226)

Over time, after consultation with the IANA, the [IETF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IETF "IETF"), [RIPE NCC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RIPE_NCC "RIPE NCC"), [APNIC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APNIC "APNIC"), and the [Federal Networking Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Networking_Council "Federal Networking Council") (FNC), the decision was made to separate the management of domain names from the management of IP numbers.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Cisco-225) Following the examples of RIPE NCC and APNIC, it was recommended that management of IP address space then administered by the InterNIC should be under the control of those that use it, specifically the ISPs, end-user organizations, corporate entities, universities, and individuals. As a result, the [American Registry for Internet Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Registry_for_Internet_Numbers "American Registry for Internet Numbers") (ARIN) was established as in December 1997, as an independent, not-for-profit corporation by direction of the [National Science Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Science_Foundation "National Science Foundation") and became the third Regional Internet Registry.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-227)

In 1998, both the IANA and remaining DNS-related InterNIC functions were reorganized under the control of [ICANN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICANN "ICANN"), a California [non-profit corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-profit_corporation "Non-profit corporation") contracted by the [United States Department of Commerce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce "United States Department of Commerce") to manage a number of Internet-related tasks. As these tasks involved technical coordination for two principal Internet name spaces (DNS names and IP addresses) created by the IETF, ICANN also signed a memorandum of understanding with the [IAB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") to define the technical work to be carried out by the Internet Assigned Numbers Authority.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-228) The management of Internet address space remained with the regional Internet registries, which collectively were defined as a supporting organization within the ICANN structure.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-229) ICANN provides central coordination for the DNS system, including policy coordination for the split registry / registrar system, with competition among registry service providers to serve each top-level-domain and multiple competing registrars offering DNS services to end-users.

### Internet Engineering Task Force

The [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF) is the largest and most visible of several loosely related ad-hoc groups that provide technical direction for the Internet, including the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB), the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG), and the [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF).

The IETF is a loosely self-organized group of international volunteers who contribute to the engineering and evolution of Internet technologies. It is the principal body engaged in the development of new Internet standard specifications. Much of the work of the IETF is organized into _Working Groups_. Standardization efforts of the Working Groups are often adopted by the Internet community, but the IETF does not control or patrol the Internet.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-230)[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-231)

The IETF grew out of quarterly meetings with U.S. government-funded researchers, starting in January 1986. Non-government representatives were invited by the fourth IETF meeting in October 1986. The concept of Working Groups was introduced at the fifth meeting in February 1987. The seventh meeting in July 1987 was the first meeting with more than one hundred attendees. In 1992, the [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society"), a professional membership society, was formed and IETF began to operate under it as an independent international standards body. The first IETF meeting outside of the United States was held in Amsterdam, the Netherlands, in July 1993. Today, the IETF meets three times per year and attendance has been as high as ca. 2,000 participants. Typically one in three IETF meetings are held in Europe or Asia. The number of non-US attendees is typically ca. 50%, even at meetings held in the United States.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-230)

The IETF is not a legal entity, has no governing board, no members, and no dues. The closest status resembling membership is being on an IETF or Working Group mailing list. IETF volunteers come from all over the world and from many different parts of the Internet community.
