The IETF works closely with and under the supervision of the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG)[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-232) and the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB).[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-233) The [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF) and the [Internet Research Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Steering_Group "Internet Research Steering Group") (IRSG), peer activities to the IETF and IESG under the general supervision of the IAB, focus on longer-term research issues.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-230)[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-234)

#### RFCs

[RFCs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments") are the main documentation for the work of the IAB, IESG, IETF, and IRTF.[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-235) Originally intended as requests for comments, RFC 1, "Host Software", was written by Steve Crocker at [UCLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UCLA "UCLA") in April 1969. These technical memos documented aspects of ARPANET development. They were edited by [Jon Postel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Postel "Jon Postel"), the first [RFC Editor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RFC_Editor "RFC Editor").[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-230)[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-RFC4844-236)

RFCs cover a wide range of information from proposed standards, draft standards, full standards, best practices, experimental protocols, history, and other informational topics.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-237) RFCs can be written by individuals or informal groups of individuals, but many are the product of a more formal Working Group. Drafts are submitted to the IESG either by individuals or by the Working Group Chair. An RFC Editor, appointed by the IAB, separate from IANA, and working in conjunction with the IESG, receives drafts from the IESG and edits, formats, and publishes them. Once an RFC is published, it is never revised. If the standard it describes changes or its information becomes obsolete, the revised standard or updated information will be re-published as a new RFC that "obsoletes" the original.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FYI17-230)[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-RFC4844-236)

### The Internet Society

The [Internet Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Society "Internet Society") (ISOC) is an international, nonprofit organization founded during 1992 "to assure the open development, evolution and use of the Internet for the benefit of all people throughout the world". With offices near Washington, DC, US, and in Geneva, Switzerland, ISOC has a membership base comprising more than 80 organizational and more than 50,000 individual members. Members also form "chapters" based on either common geographical location or special interests. There are currently more than 90 chapters around the world.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-isoc.org-238)

ISOC provides financial and organizational support to and promotes the work of the standards settings bodies for which it is the organizational home: the [Internet Engineering Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Task_Force "Internet Engineering Task Force") (IETF), the [Internet Architecture Board](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Architecture_Board "Internet Architecture Board") (IAB), the [Internet Engineering Steering Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Engineering_Steering_Group "Internet Engineering Steering Group") (IESG), and the [Internet Research Task Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Research_Task_Force "Internet Research Task Force") (IRTF). ISOC also promotes understanding and appreciation of the [Internet model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_model "Internet model") of open, transparent processes and consensus-based decision-making.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-239)

### Globalization and Internet governance in the 21st century

Since the 1990s, the [Internet's governance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_governance "Internet governance") and organization has been of global importance to governments, commerce, civil society, and individuals. The organizations which held control of certain technical aspects of the Internet were the successors of the old ARPANET oversight and the current decision-makers in the day-to-day technical aspects of the network. While recognized as the administrators of certain aspects of the Internet, their roles and their decision-making authority are limited and subject to increasing international scrutiny and increasing objections. These objections have led to the ICANN removing themselves from relationships with first the [University of Southern California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Southern_California "University of Southern California") in 2000,[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-240) and in September 2009 gaining autonomy from the US government by the ending of its longstanding agreements, although some contractual obligations with the U.S. Department of Commerce continued.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-241)[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-242)[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-243) Finally, on October 1, 2016, ICANN ended its contract with the United States Department of Commerce National Telecommunications and Information Administration (NTIA), allowing oversight to pass to the global Internet community.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-244)

The IETF, with financial and organizational support from the Internet Society, continues to serve as the Internet's ad-hoc standards body and issues [Request for Comments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Request_for_Comments "Request for Comments").

In November 2005, the [World Summit on the Information Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Summit_on_the_Information_Society "World Summit on the Information Society"), held in [Tunis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunis "Tunis"), called for an [Internet Governance Forum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Governance_Forum "Internet Governance Forum") (IGF) to be convened by [United Nations Secretary General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Secretary_General "United Nations Secretary General"). The IGF opened an ongoing, non-binding conversation among stakeholders representing governments, the private sector, civil society, and the technical and academic communities about the future of Internet governance. The first IGF meeting was held in October/November 2006 with follow up meetings annually thereafter.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-245) Since WSIS, the term "Internet governance" has been broadened beyond narrow technical concerns to include a wider range of Internet-related policy issues.[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-246)[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-247)

[Tim Berners-Lee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee "Tim Berners-Lee"), inventor of the web, was becoming concerned about threats to the web's future and in November 2009 at the IGF in Washington DC launched the [World Wide Web Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web_Foundation "World Wide Web Foundation") (WWWF) to campaign to make the web a safe and empowering tool for the good of humanity with access to all.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-BBC20080915-248)[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ARST20091117-249) In November 2019 at the IGF in Berlin, Berners-Lee and the WWWF went on to launch the _[Contract for the Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contract\_for\_the\_Web "Contract for the Web")_, a campaign initiative to persuade governments, companies and citizens to commit to nine principles to stop "misuse" with the warning "If we don't act now - and act together - to prevent the web being misused by those who want to exploit, divide and undermine, we are at risk of squandering" (its potential for good).[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-CNA20191125-250)

## Politicization of the Internet

Due to its prominence and immediacy as an effective means of mass communication, the Internet has also become more [politicized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politicized "Politicized") as it has grown. This has led in turn, to discourses and activities that would once have taken place in other ways, migrating to being mediated by internet.

Examples include political activities such as [public protest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_protest "Mass protest") and [canvassing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canvassing "Canvassing") of support and [votes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vote "Vote"), but also:

*   The spreading of ideas and opinions;
*   Recruitment of followers, and "coming together" of members of the public, for ideas, products, and causes;
*   Providing and widely distributing and sharing information that might be deemed sensitive or relates to [whistleblowing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whistleblowing "Whistleblowing") (and efforts by specific countries to prevent this by [censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_censorship "Internet censorship"));
*   [Criminal activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crime "Crime") and [terrorism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrorism "Terrorism") (and resulting [law enforcement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_enforcement "Law enforcement") use, together with its facilitation by [mass surveillance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_surveillance "Mass surveillance"));
*   Politically motivated [fake news](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fake_news "Fake news").

## Net neutrality

Main article: [Net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality")

![Image 20: Globe icon.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/60px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png)The examples and perspective in this section **may not represent a [worldwide view](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias "Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias") of the subject**. You may [improve this section](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_Internet&action=edit), discuss the issue on the [talk page](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:History_of_the_Internet "Talk:History of the Internet"), or create a new section, as appropriate._(April 2015)_ _([Learn how and when to remove this message](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Maintenance\_template\_removal "Help:Maintenance template removal"))_

On April 23, 2014, the [Federal Communications Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission "Federal Communications Commission") (FCC) was reported to be considering a new rule that would permit [Internet service providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") to offer content providers a faster track to send content, thus reversing their earlier [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality") position.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140423-251)[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140424a-252)[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140511-253) A possible solution to net neutrality concerns may be [municipal broadband](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipal_broadband "Municipal broadband"), according to [Professor Susan Crawford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Susan_P._Crawford "Susan P. Crawford"), a legal and technology expert at [Harvard Law School](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harvard_Law_School "Harvard Law School").[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140428-254) On May 15, 2014, the FCC decided to consider two options regarding Internet services: first, permit fast and slow broadband lanes, thereby compromising net neutrality; and second, reclassify broadband as a telecommunication service, thereby preserving net neutrality.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140515a-255)[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20140515b-256) On November 10, 2014, [President Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") recommended the FCC reclassify broadband Internet service as a telecommunications service in order to preserve [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality").[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20141110-EW-257)[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20141114-258)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WRD-20150121-DAS-259) On January 16, 2015, [Republicans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States) "Republican Party (United States)") presented legislation, in the form of a [U.S. Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Congress "U.S. Congress")[HR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives "United States House of Representatives")[discussion draft bill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_bills_in_the_114th_United_States_Congress "List of bills in the 114th United States Congress"), that makes concessions to net neutrality but prohibits the FCC from accomplishing the goal or enacting any further regulation affecting [Internet service providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_service_provider "Internet service provider") (ISPs).[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150120-JW-260)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-HG-20150116-261) On January 31, 2015, [AP News](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AP_News "AP News") reported that the FCC will present the notion of applying ("with some caveats") [Title II (common carrier)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_carrier#Telecommunications "Common carrier") of the [Communications Act of 1934](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934 "Communications Act of 1934") to the internet in a vote expected on February 26, 2015.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150202a-262)[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150202b-263)[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150131-264)[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WP-20150102-265)[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150102-266) Adoption of this notion would reclassify internet service from one of information to one of [telecommunications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications_service_provider "Telecommunications service provider")[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150204-267) and, according to [Tom Wheeler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Wheeler "Tom Wheeler"), chairman of the FCC, ensure [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality").[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-WRD-20150204-268)[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150206-269) The FCC is expected to enforce net neutrality in its vote, according to _[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_New\_York\_Times "The New York Times")_.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150224-270)[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150225-271)

On February 26, 2015, the FCC ruled in favor of [net neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality "Net neutrality") by applying [Title II (common carrier)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_carrier#Telecommunications "Common carrier") of the [Communications Act of 1934](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934 "Communications Act of 1934") and [Section 706](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telecommunications_policy_of_the_United_States#Broadband_deployment_policy_objectives "Telecommunications policy of the United States") of the [Telecommunications act of 1996](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934#Telecommunications_Act_of_1996 "Communications Act of 1934") to the Internet.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FCC-20150226-272)[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150226-273)[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-AP-20150225-274) The FCC chairman, [Tom Wheeler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Wheeler "Tom Wheeler"), commented, "This is no more a plan to regulate the Internet than the [First Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution "First Amendment to the United States Constitution") is a plan to regulate free speech. They both stand for the same concept."[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-HP-20150226-275)

On March 12, 2015, the FCC released the specific details of the net neutrality rules.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150312a-276)[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-NYT-20150312b-277)[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FCC-20150315-278) On April 13, 2015, the FCC published the final rule on its new "[Net Neutrality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_neutrality_in_the_United_States "Net neutrality in the United States")" regulations.[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-CNET-20150413-279)[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-FR-20150413-280)

On December 14, 2017, the FCC repealed their March 12, 2015 decision by a 3–2 vote regarding net neutrality rules.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-281)

## Use and culture

### Email and Usenet

[Email](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Email "Email") has often been called the [killer application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killer_application "Killer application") of the Internet. It predates the Internet, and was a crucial tool in creating it. Email started in 1965 as a way for multiple users of a [time-sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time-sharing "Time-sharing")[mainframe computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainframe_computer "Mainframe computer") to communicate. Although the history is undocumented, among the first systems to have such a facility were the [System Development Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/System_Development_Corporation "System Development Corporation") (SDC) [Q32](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AN/FSQ-32 "AN/FSQ-32") and the [Compatible Time-Sharing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatible_Time-Sharing_System "Compatible Time-Sharing System") (CTSS) at MIT.[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-282)

The ARPANET computer network made a large contribution to the evolution of electronic mail. An experimental inter-system transferred mail on the ARPANET shortly after its creation.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-283) In 1971 [Ray Tomlinson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Tomlinson "Ray Tomlinson") created what was to become the standard Internet electronic mail addressing format, using the [@ sign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/@ "@") to separate mailbox names from host names.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-284)

A number of protocols were developed to deliver messages among groups of time-sharing computers over alternative transmission systems, such as [UUCP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UUCP "UUCP") and [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM")'s [VNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VNET "VNET") email system. Email could be passed this way between a number of networks, including [ARPANET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARPANET "ARPANET"), [BITNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BITNET "BITNET") and [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET"), as well as to hosts connected directly to other sites via UUCP. See the [history of SMTP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SMTP#History "SMTP") protocol.

In addition, UUCP allowed the publication of text files that could be read by many others. The News software developed by Steve Daniel and [Tom Truscott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Truscott "Tom Truscott") in 1979 was used to distribute news and bulletin board-like messages. This quickly grew into discussion groups, known as [newsgroups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newsgroup "Newsgroup"), on a wide range of topics. On ARPANET and NSFNET similar discussion groups would form via [mailing lists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_mailing_list "Electronic mailing list"), discussing both technical issues and more culturally focused topics (such as science fiction, discussed on the sflovers mailing list).

During the early years of the Internet, email and similar mechanisms were also fundamental to allow people to access resources that were not available due to the absence of online connectivity. UUCP was often used to distribute files using the 'alt.binary' groups. Also, [FTP e-mail gateways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FTPmail "FTPmail") allowed people that lived outside the US and Europe to download files using ftp commands written inside email messages. The file was encoded, broken in pieces and sent by email; the receiver had to reassemble and decode it later, and it was the only way for people living overseas to download items such as the earlier Linux versions using the slow dial-up connections available at the time. After the popularization of the Web and the HTTP protocol such tools were slowly abandoned.

### File sharing

Main articles: [File sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_sharing "File sharing"), [Peer-to-peer file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer-to-peer_file_sharing "Peer-to-peer file sharing"), and [Timeline of file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_file_sharing "Timeline of file sharing")

Resource or file sharing has been an important activity on computer networks from well before the Internet was established and was supported in a variety of ways including [bulletin board systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulletin_board_systems "Bulletin board systems") (1978), [Usenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usenet "Usenet") (1980), [Kermit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kermit_(software) "Kermit (software)") (1981), and many others. The [File Transfer Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_Transfer_Protocol "File Transfer Protocol") (FTP) for use on the Internet was standardized in 1985 and is still in use today.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-285) A variety of tools were developed to aid the use of FTP by helping users discover files they might want to transfer, including the [Wide Area Information Server](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Area_Information_Server "Wide Area Information Server") (WAIS) in 1991, [Gopher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gopher_(protocol) "Gopher (protocol)") in 1991, [Archie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archie_search_engine "Archie search engine") in 1991, [Veronica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veronica_(search_engine) "Veronica (search engine)") in 1992, [Jughead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jughead_(search_engine) "Jughead (search engine)") in 1993, [Internet Relay Chat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat "Internet Relay Chat") (IRC) in 1988, and eventually the [World Wide Web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web "World Wide Web") (WWW) in 1991 with [Web directories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_directories "Web directories") and [Web search engines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_search_engines "Web search engines").

In 1999, [Napster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napster "Napster") became the first [peer-to-peer file sharing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peer-to-peer_file_sharing "Peer-to-peer file sharing") system.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-286) Napster used a central server for indexing and peer discovery, but the storage and transfer of files was decentralized. A variety of peer-to-peer file sharing programs and services with different levels of decentralization and [anonymity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anonymity_application "Anonymity application") followed, including: [Gnutella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gnutella "Gnutella"), [eDonkey2000](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EDonkey2000 "EDonkey2000"), and [Freenet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freenet "Freenet") in 2000, [FastTrack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FastTrack "FastTrack"), [Kazaa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kazaa "Kazaa"), [Limewire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limewire "Limewire"), and [BitTorrent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BitTorrent_(software) "BitTorrent (software)") in 2001, and Poisoned in 2003.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-287)

All of these tools are general purpose and can be used to share a wide variety of content, but sharing of music files, software, and later movies and videos are major uses.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-288) And while some of this sharing is legal, large portions are not. Lawsuits and other legal actions caused Napster in 2001, eDonkey2000 in 2005, [Kazaa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kazaa "Kazaa") in 2006, and Limewire in 2010 to shut down or refocus their efforts.[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-289)[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-290)[The Pirate Bay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay "The Pirate Bay"), founded in Sweden in 2003, continues despite a [trial and appeal in 2009 and 2010](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay_trial "The Pirate Bay trial") that resulted in jail terms and large fines for several of its founders.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-291) File sharing remains contentious and controversial with charges of theft of [intellectual property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intellectual_property "Intellectual property") on the one hand and charges of [censorship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Censorship "Censorship") on the other.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-292)[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-293)

### File hosting services

Main article: [File-hosting service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File-hosting_service "File-hosting service")

[![Image 21: icon](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/60px-Question_book-new.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg)This section **relies excessively on [references](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability") to [primary sources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources "Wikipedia:No original research")**. Please improve this section by adding [secondary or tertiary sources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources "Wikipedia:No original research"). 

_Find sources:_["History of the Internet"](https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&q=%22History+of+the+Internet%22)–[news](https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&q=%22History+of+the+Internet%22+-wikipedia&tbs=ar:1)**·**[newspapers](https://www.google.com/search?&q=%22History+of+the+Internet%22&tbs=bkt:s&tbm=bks)**·**[books](https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&q=%22History+of+the+Internet%22+-wikipedia)**·**[scholar](https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22History+of+the+Internet%22)**·**[JSTOR](https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22History+of+the+Internet%22&acc=on&wc=on)_(October 2024)_ _([Learn how and when to remove this message](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Maintenance\_template\_removal "Help:Maintenance template removal"))_

File hosting allowed for people to expand their computer's hard drives and "host" their files on a server. Most file hosting services offer free storage, as well as larger storage amount for a fee. These services have greatly expanded the internet for business and personal use.

[Google Drive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Drive "Google Drive"), launched on April 24, 2012, has become the most popular file hosting service. Google Drive allows users to store, edit, and share files with themselves and other users. Not only does this application allow for file editing, hosting, and sharing. It also acts as Google's own free-to-access office programs, such as [Google Docs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Docs "Google Docs"), [Google Slides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Slides "Google Slides"), and [Google Sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Sheets "Google Sheets"). This application served as a useful tool for University professors and students, as well as those who are in need of [Cloud storage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_storage "Cloud storage").[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-294)[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-295)

[Dropbox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dropbox_(service)#History "Dropbox (service)"), released in June 2007 is a similar file hosting service that allows users to keep all of their files in a folder on their computer, which is synced with Dropbox's servers. This differs from Google Drive as it is not web-browser based. Now, Dropbox works to keep workers and files in sync and efficient.[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-296)

[Mega](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mega_(service) "Mega (service)"), having over 200 million users, is an encrypted storage and communication system that offers users free and paid storage, with an emphasis on privacy.[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-297) Being three of the largest file hosting services, Google Drive, Dropbox, and Mega all represent the core ideas and values of these services.

## Online piracy

Main article: [Online piracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Online_piracy "Online piracy")

The earliest form of online piracy began with a P2P (peer to peer) music sharing service named [Napster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napster "Napster"), launched in 1999. Sites like [LimeWire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LimeWire "LimeWire"), [The Pirate Bay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pirate_Bay "The Pirate Bay"), and [BitTorrent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BitTorrent "BitTorrent") allowed for anyone to engage in online piracy, sending ripples through the media industry. With online piracy came a change in the media industry as a whole.[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-298)

## Mobile telephone data traffic

See also: [Mobile web](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_web "Mobile web")

Total global mobile data traffic reached 588 exabytes during 2020,[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ReferenceA-299) a 150-fold increase from 3.86 exabytes/year in 2010.[[300]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-300) Most recently, smartphones accounted for 95% of this mobile data traffic with video accounting for 66% by type of data.[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-ReferenceA-299) Mobile traffic travels by radio frequency to the closest cell phone tower and its base station where the radio signal is converted into an optical signal that is transmitted over high-capacity optical networking systems that convey the information to data centers. The optical backbones enable much of this traffic as well as a host of emerging mobile services including the Internet of things, 3-D virtual reality, gaming and autonomous vehicles. The most popular mobile phone application is texting, of which 2.1 trillion messages were logged in 2020.[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-301) The texting phenomenon began on December 3, 1992, when Neil Papworth sent the first text message of "Merry Christmas" over a commercial cell phone network to the CEO of Vodafone.[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-302)

The first mobile phone with Internet connectivity was the [Nokia 9000 Communicator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nokia_9000_Communicator "Nokia 9000 Communicator"), launched in Finland in 1996. The viability of Internet services access on mobile phones was limited until prices came down from that model, and network providers started to develop systems and services conveniently accessible on phones. [NTT DoCoMo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo "NTT DoCoMo") in Japan launched the first mobile Internet service, [i-mode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I-mode "I-mode"), in 1999 and this is considered the birth of the mobile phone Internet services. In 2001, the mobile phone email system by Research in Motion (now [BlackBerry Limited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BlackBerry_Limited "BlackBerry Limited")) for their [BlackBerry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BlackBerry "BlackBerry") product was launched in America. To make efficient use of the small screen and [tiny keypad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_keypad "Telephone keypad") and one-handed operation typical of mobile phones, a specific document and networking model was created for mobile devices, the [Wireless Application Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wireless_Application_Protocol "Wireless Application Protocol") (WAP). Most mobile device Internet services operate using WAP. The growth of mobile phone services was initially a primarily Asian phenomenon with Japan, South Korea and Taiwan all soon finding the majority of their Internet users accessing resources by phone rather than by PC.[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-303) Developing countries followed, with India, South Africa, Kenya, the Philippines, and Pakistan all reporting that the majority of their domestic users accessed the Internet from a mobile phone rather than a PC. The European and North American use of the Internet was influenced by a large installed base of personal computers, and the growth of mobile phone Internet access was more gradual, but had reached national penetration levels of 20–30% in most Western countries.[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-DasguptaLall2001-304) The cross-over occurred in 2008, when more Internet access devices were mobile phones than personal computers. In many parts of the developing world, the ratio is as much as 10 mobile phone users to one PC user.[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-305)

## Growth in demand

Global Internet traffic continues to grow at a rapid rate, rising 23% from 2020 to 2021[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-306) when the number of active Internet users reached 4.66 billion people, representing half of the global population. Further demand for data, and the capacity to satisfy this demand, are forecast to increase to 717 terabits per second in 2021.[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-307) This capacity stems from the [optical amplification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_amplifier "Optical amplifier") and [WDM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength-division_multiplexing "Wavelength-division multiplexing") systems that are the common basis of virtually every metro, regional, national, international and submarine telecommunications networks.[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-308) These [optical networking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_networking "Optical networking") systems have been installed throughout the 5 billion kilometers of [fiber optic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber_optic "Fiber optic") lines deployed around the world.[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-309) Continued growth in traffic is expected for the foreseeable future from a combination of new users, increased mobile phone adoption, machine-to-machine connections, connected homes, 5G devices and the burgeoning requirement for cloud and Internet services such as [Amazon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_(company) "Amazon (company)"), [Facebook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Facebook "Facebook"), [Apple Music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Music "Apple Music") and [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube").

## Historiography

Further information: [Protocol Wars §Historiography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protocol_Wars#Historiography "Protocol Wars")

There are nearly insurmountable problems in supplying a [historiography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historiography "Historiography") of the Internet's development. The process of digitization represents a twofold challenge both for historiography in general and, in particular, for historical communication research.[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-310) A sense of the difficulty in documenting early developments that led to the internet can be gathered from the quote:

> "The Arpanet period is somewhat well documented because the corporation in charge – [BBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBN_Technologies "BBN Technologies") – left a physical record. Moving into the [NSFNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSFNET "NSFNET") era, it became an extraordinarily decentralized process. The record exists in people's basements, in closets. ... So much of what happened was done verbally and on the basis of individual trust."

— [Doug Gale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doug_Gale "Doug Gale") (2007)[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-311)

Notable works on the subject include a book by journalists [Katie Hafner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Katie_Hafner "Katie Hafner") and Matthew Lyon, _Where Wizards Stay Up Late: The Origins Of The Internet_ (1996); and professor [Janet Abbate's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Janet_Abbate "Janet Abbate") book _Inventing the Internet_ (2000).[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-312)

Most scholarship and literature on the Internet lists ARPANET as the prior network that was iterated on and studied to create it,[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-313) although other early computer networks and experiments existed alongside or before ARPANET.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-Roberts1978-40)

Such histories of the Internet have since been criticized as [teleologies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleology "Teleology") and [Whig history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whig_history "Whig history"); that is, they take the present to be the end point toward which history has been unfolding based on a single cause:[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-314)

> In the case of Internet history, the epoch-making event is usually said to be the demonstration of the 4-node ARPANET network in 1969. From that single happening the global Internet developed.

— [Martin Campbell-Kelly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Campbell-Kelly "Martin Campbell-Kelly"), Daniel D. Garcia-Swartz

In addition to these characteristics, historians have cited methodological problems arising in their work:

> "Internet history" ... tends to be too close to its sources. Many Internet pioneers are alive, active, and eager to shape the histories that describe their accomplishments. Many museums and historians are equally eager to interview the pioneers and to publicize their stories.

— Andrew L. Russell (2012)[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_note-315)

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-Abbatep3_1-0)**[Abbate 1999](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#CITEREFAbbate1999), p.[3](https://books.google.com/books?id=9BfZxFZpElwC&pg=PA3) "The manager of the ARPANET project, Lawrence Roberts, assembled a large team of computer scientists ... and he drew on the ideas of network experimenters in the United States and the United Kingdom. Cerf and Kahn also enlisted the help of computer scientists from England, France and the United States"
2.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-:3_2-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-:3_2-1)["The Computer History Museum, SRI International, and BBN Celebrate the 40th Anniversary of First ARPANET Transmission, Precursor to Today's Internet"](https://web.archive.org/web/20190329134941/https://www.sri.com/newsroom/press-releases/computer-history-museum-sri-international-and-bbn-celebrate-40th-anniversary). SRI International. October 27, 2009. Archived from [the original](https://www.sri.com/newsroom/press-releases/computer-history-museum-sri-international-and-bbn-celebrate-40th-anniversary) on March 29, 2019. Retrieved September 25, 2017. But the ARPANET itself had now become an island, with no links to the other networks that had sprung up. By the early 1970s, researchers in France, the UK, and the U.S. began developing ways of connecting networks to each other, a process known as internetworking.
3.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-:4_3-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-:4_3-1)by Vinton Cerf, as told to Bernard Aboba (1993). ["How the Internet Came to Be"](https://web.archive.org/web/20170926042220/http://elk.informatik.hs-augsburg.de/tmp/cdrom-oss/CerfHowInternetCame2B.html). Archived from [the original](http://elk.informatik.hs-augsburg.de/tmp/cdrom-oss/CerfHowInternetCame2B.html) on September 26, 2017. Retrieved September 25, 2017. We began doing concurrent implementations at Stanford, BBN, and University College London. So effort at developing the Internet protocols was international from the beginning.
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-4)**["The Untold Internet"](https://www.internethalloffame.org/blog/2015/10/19/untold-internet). _Internet Hall of Fame_. October 19, 2015. Retrieved April 3, 2020. many of the milestones that led to the development of the modern Internet are already familiar to many of us: the genesis of the ARPANET, the implementation of the standard network protocol TCP/IP, the growth of LANs (Large Area Networks), the invention of DNS (the Domain Name System), and the adoption of American legislation that funded U.S. Internet expansion—which helped fuel global network access—to name just a few.
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-5)**["Study into UK IPv4 and IPv6 allocations"](https://www.ofcom.org.uk/__data/assets/pdf_file/0031/37795/rtfm.pdf)(PDF). _Reid Technical Facilities Management LLP_. 2014. As the network continued to grow, the model of central co-ordination by a contractor funded by the US government became unsustainable. Organisations were using IP-based networking even if they were not directly connected to the ARPAnet. They needed to get globally unique IP addresses. The nature of the ARPAnet was also changing as it was no longer limited to organisations working on ARPA-funded contracts. The US National Science Foundation set up a national IP-based backbone network, NSFnet, so that its grant-holders could be interconnected to supercomputer centres, universities and various national/regional academic/research networks, including ARPAnet. That resulting network of networks was the beginning of today's Internet.

27.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-Pelkey6.1a_27-0)**Pelkey, James L. As Kahn recalls: ... Paul Baran's contributions ... I also think Paul was motivated almost entirely by voice considerations. If you look at what he wrote, he was talking about switches that were low-cost electronics. The idea of putting powerful computers in these locations hadn't quite occurred to him as being cost effective. So the idea of computer switches was missing. The whole notion of protocols didn't exist at that time. And the idea of computer-to-computer communications was really a secondary concern.

[ISSN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISSN_(identifier) "ISSN (identifier)")[0001-0782](https://search.worldcat.org/issn/0001-0782). In his first draft dated Nov. 10, 1965 [5], Davies forecast today's "killer app" for his new communication service: "The greatest traffic could only come if the public used this means for everyday purposes such as shopping... People sending enquiries and placing orders for goods of all kinds will make up a large section of the traffic... Business use of the telephone may be reduced by the growth of the kind of service we contemplate."
43.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-43)**Davies, D. W. (1966). ["Proposal for a Digital Communication Network"](https://www.dcs.gla.ac.uk/~wpc/grcs/Davies05.pdf)(PDF). Computer developments in the distant future might result in one type of network being able to carry speech and digital messages efficiently.
44.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-44)**Roberts, Dr. Lawrence G. (May 1995). Archived from [the original](http://www.packet.cc/files/arpanet-computernet.html) on March 24, 2016. Retrieved April 13, 2016. Then in June 1966, Davies wrote a second internal paper, "Proposal for a Digital Communication Network" In which he coined the word packet,- a small sub part of the message the user wants to send, and also introduced the concept of an "Interface computer" to sit between the user equipment and the packet network.

"As well as the packet switched network actually built at NPL for communication between their local computing facilities, some simulation experiments have been performed on larger networks. A summary of this work is reported in [69]. The work was carried out to investigate networks of a size capable of providing data communications facilities to most of the U.K. ... Experiments were then carried out using a method of flow control devised by Davies [70] called 'isarithmic' flow control. ... The simulation work carried out at NPL has, in many respects, been more realistic than most of the ARPA network theoretical studies."

"Many of the theoretical studies of the performance and design of the ARPA Network were developments of earlier work by Kleinrock ... Although these works concerned message switching networks, they were the basis for a lot of the ARPA network investigations ... The intention of the work of Kleinrock [in 1961] was to analyse the performance of store and forward networks ... Kleinrock [in 1970] extended the theoretical approaches of [his 1961 work] to the early ARPA network."

In mathematical modelling use is made of the theories of queueing processes and of flows in networks, describing the performance of the network in a set of equations. ... The analytic method has been used with success by Kleinrock and others, but only if important simplifying assumptions are made. ... It is heartening in Kleinrock's work to see the good correspondence achieved between the results of analytic methods and those of simulation.

89.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-Green_2010_31_89-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-Green_2010_31_89-1)Green, Lelia (2010). The original ARPANET design had made data integrity part of the IMP's store-and-forward role, but Cyclades end-to-end protocol greatly simplified the packet switching operations of the network. ... The idea was to adopt several principles from Cyclades and invert the ARPANET model to minimise international differences.
90.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Internet#cite_ref-Bennett20092_90-0)**Bennett, Richard (September 2009). ["Designed for Change: End-to-End Arguments, Internet Innovation, and the Net Neutrality Debate"](https://www.itif.org/files/2009-designed-for-change.pdf)(PDF). Information Technology and Innovation Foundation. pp.7, 9, 11. Retrieved September 11, 2017. Two significant packet networks preceded the TCP/IP Internet: ARPANET and CYCLADES. The designers of the Internet borrowed heavily from these systems, especially CYCLADES ... The first end-to-end research network was CYCLA DES, designed by Louis Pouzin at IRIA in France with the support of BBN's Dave Walden and Alex McKenzie and deployed beginning in 1972.

Its simplicity and efficiency pointed the way to a network that could connect not just dozens of machines, but millions of them. It captured the imagination of Dr Cerf and Dr Kahn, who included aspects of its design in the protocols that now power the internet.
